  THE  UPPER  HAND  WITHOUT  looking  out  the  window,  Herbert  Watkins  could  tell  that  his  train  was  approaching  his  station.  He  folded  his  newspaper,  put  on  his  hat,  and  joined  the  line  of  expressionless  commuters  moving  down  the  aisle  toward  the  door.  The  train  joltedto  a  stop,  and  the  com-  mutersfanned  out  mechanically  across  the  station  plaza  to  where  their  wives  or  automobiles  were  waiting  in  accus-  tomed  places.  Watkins  slipped  in  be-  hind  the  wheel  of  his  car  and  simul-  taneously  kissed  his  wife,  tossed  the  paper  in  her  lap,  and  put  the  car  in  gear.  He  nosed  it  out  into  the  line  of  traffic.  &quot;Donald  ran  away  from  home  to-  day,&quot;  his  wife  said.  Donald  was  their  nine-year-old  son.  &quot;Oh?&quot;  said  Watkins.  &quot;Where'd  he  0.9”  &quot;I  don't  know,&quot;  she  said.  &quot;He  just  said  he  was  running  away,  and  I  figured  it  was  best  to  let  him  go.&quot;  &quot;Did  he  give  any  reason?&quot;  Watkins  asked.  &quot;No,&quot;  she  said,  &quot;but  he  hadn't  said  a  word  to  anyone  since  last  night.  I  imagine  that  was  what  did  it.&quot;  &quot;Oh,&quot;  said  Watkins.  &quot;Well,  I'm  sorry  about  that,  but  there  was  nothing  else  I  could  do.&quot;  &quot;It  was  a  pretty  stiff  punishment,  didn't  you  think?&quot;  &quot;He  was  caught  in  a  deliberate  lie.  I  am  not  going  to  have  any  child  of  mine  think  he  can  get  away  with  lying.&quot;  &quot;I  know,&quot;  she  said.  &quot;'There  might  have  been  something  else  you  could  have  done,  though.  At  any  rate,  I  .  figured  it  was  better  to  let  him  go,  and  get  it  out  of  his  system.  He'll  be  back  by  dinner,  I'm  sure.&quot;  &quot;I  guess  so,&quot;  said  Watkins.  &quot;Every  kid  has  to  run  away  from  home  once.&quot;  He  smiled.  &quot;Re-  member  Louise?&quot;  Their  daugh-  ter  Louise,  who  was  now  six-  teen,  had  run  away  for  three  hours  when  she  was  ten.  She  had  come  home  and  hidden  in  her  room  until  nightfall,  to  make  it  appear  that  she  had  been  away  longer.  His  wife  smiled,  too.  &quot;&quot;Yes,&quot;  she  said.  &quot;And  I  remember  doing  the  same  thing  when  I  was  her  age.&quot;  &quot;I  think  you  were  right  to  let  Don-  ald  go,&quot;  Watkins  said.  &quot;If  you'd  tried  to  talk  him  out  of  it,  he'd  have  felt  that  he  had  the  upper  hand.&quot;  &quot;I  thought  for  a  minute  of  telling  him  to  wait  until  you  got  home,&quot;  said     his  wife.  &quot;But  then  I  just  said  if  that  was  what  he  wanted  to  do,  he'd  better  do  it.  I  think  it  kind  of  surprised  him.&quot;  &quot;Of  course,&quot;  said  Watkins.  &quot;He  wanted  you  to  argue  with  him.&quot;  He  drove  for  a  while  in  silence,  and  then  said,  &quot;W  hat  time  did  all  this  happen,  by  the  way?&quot;  &quot;About  noon,&quot;  she  said.  &quot;I  asked  him  what  he  wanted  for  lunch,  and  he  said  not  to  bother,  that  he  was  leaving  home.  He'd  packed  his  small  suitcase,  but  I  don't  know  what  he'd  put  in  it.&quot;  &quot;Noon,&quot;  Watkins  said.  &quot;I  bet  he'll  be  home  before  dinner.&quot;  They  held  up  dinner  for  fifteen  or  twenty  minutes,  then  went  ahead  with  it,  although  neither  of  them  was  very  hungry.  It  was  darkby  the  timethey  finished,  and  theytook  their  coffee  into  the  living  room.  Louise  had  eaten  early,  andhadgone  out  with  a  young  man  called  Pin-Head  Gerlack-W  at-  kins  had  neverbeen  able  to  find  out  his  Christian  name-who  looked  about  twelve  but  was  actually  seventeen.  For  no  particular  reason,  Watkins  had  told  Louise  he  would  like  her  home  by  twelve,  and  she  gave  an  elaborate  sigh  of  martyrdom  and  flounced  out  the  door.  Watkins  immediately  wished  he  hadn't  spoken,  and  then  wondered  what  had  made  him.  Now  he  put  down  his  coffee  cup,  and  went  to  a  window  and  looked  out.  It  was  completely  dark,  but  he  could  see  the  trees  silhouetted  against  the  night  sky,  and  he  wondered  if  there  was  going  to  be  a  moon.  He  remem-  bered  there  being  a  moon  a  couple  of  nights  previously,  buthe  couldn't  re-  member  whether  it  was  an  old  one  or  a  new  one.  He  turned  away  from  the  window.  His  wife,  who  was  working  on  a  needle-  point  design,  looked  up  at  him.  &quot;He  ought  to  be  back  any  time  now,&quot;  she  said.  &quot;Yes,&quot;  said  Watkins.  He  looked  at  his  watch.  &quot;If  he  isn't  back  in,  say,  a  half  hour,  I  suppose  we  ought  to  call  a  couple  of  people.  He  may  have  gone  to  the  Lefferts'  or  the  Talberts'  or  somewhere,  and  be  plan-  ning  to  spend  the  night.  I  wouldn't  want  him  to  bother  them.&quot;  &quot;I  hadn't  thought  of  that,&quot;  said  his  wife.  &quot;I  suppose  you're  right.&quot;  She  went  on  with  her  needlepoint,  and  Watkins  made  himself  a  highball.  He  looked  at  his  watch  at  least  twelve  times  in  the  next  half  hour,  and  then  sat  down  by  the  telephone.  &quot;All  right,&quot;  he  said.  &quot;Whom  shall  I  call        first?  Where's  the  most  likely  place  for  him  to  be?&quot;  &quot;The  Talberts',  I  guess,&quot;  said  his  wife.  &quot;He  spends  more  time  with  Tom-  my  Talbert  than  anyone  else.&quot;  She  kept  her  eyes  on  her  needlepoint,  and  sewed  more  quickly.  Watkins  dialled  the  Talberts'  num-  ber,  and  after  two  rings  George  Talbert  answered.  &quot;Hello,  George,&quot;  Watkins  said.  &quot;Herb  Watkins.&quot;  His  voice  was  casual,  and  he  made  it  sound  faintly  amused.  &quot;George,  you  haven't  by  any  chance  got  our  youngest  unmarried  over  there,  have  you?  He  doesn't  seem  to  be  here.&quot;  &quot;No,  we  haven't,  Herb,&quot;  said  Tal-  bert.  &quot;I  haven't  seen  him  in  a  couple  of  days.&quot;  &quot;Uh-huh.  Well,  thanks  anyway,  George.&quot;  Watkins  paused.  &quot;Oh,&quot;  he  said.  &quot;Would  you  check  with  Tommy,  and  see  if  he  saw  him  this  afternoon?  Just-  you  know.  It  might  give  us  a  hint.  Where  to  call.&quot;  &quot;Sure,  Herb,&quot;  said  Talbert.  &quot;Just  a  minute.&quot;  He  must  have  put  his  hand  over  the  mouthpiece,  for  Watkins  heard  metallic,  distorted  sounds  as  he  called  his  son.  There  was  a  pause,  and  then  Talbert  said,  &quot;No,  Herb.  He  hasn't  seen  him,  either.&quot;  &quot;O.K.,  George.  Thanks,&quot;  said  Wat-  kins.  &quot;I'm  sorry  to  have  bothered  you.&quot;  &quot;That's  all  right,&quot;  Talbert  said.  &quot;Hope  you  find  him.&quot;  &quot;Thanks.&quot;  Watkins  hung  up,  and  looked  at  his  wife.  &quot;O.K.,&quot;  he  said.  &quot;Now  where?&quot;  &quot;Oh,  I  guess  the  Tompkins',&quot;  she  said.  &quot;Or  the  Lefferts'.  He  could  be  any  number  of  places.&quot;  &quot;Yes,&quot;  said  Watkins.  &quot;I  know.&quot;  He  dialled  the  Tompkins'  number,  and  Ethel  Tompkins  answered.  &quot;Hello,  Ethel,&quot;  he  said.  &quot;Herb  Watkins.  Is  Donald  there,  by  any  chance?&quot;  &quot;No,  he  isn't,  Herb,&quot;  she  said.  &quot;Have  you  lost  him?&quot;  &quot;Well,  not  exactly,&quot;  said  Watkins.  &quot;It's  just  that  we  can't-we  don't  know  where  he  is.&quot;  &quot;Have  you  tried  the  Talberts?&quot;  she  asked.  (‘Yes')’  &quot;When  did  you  last  see  him?&quot;  &quot;It  was  around  noon,  I  guess,&quot;  Wat-  kins  said.  &quot;He-left  the  house  before  lunch,  and  that's  all  we  know.&quot;  &quot;Well,  I  haven't  seen  him  all  day,&quot;  she  said,  &quot;but  I'll  let  you  know  if  I  hear  anything.  Did  you  try  the  Gun-  deckers?&quot;  &quot;No,&quot;  said  Watki there,&quot;  she  said.  &quot;I'd  certainly  try  them  if  I  were  you.&quot;  &quot;Thanks,  Ethel,&quot;  Watkins  said,  with  a  sudden  feeling  of  cheer.  &quot;Thanks  a  lot.&quot;  ALF  an  hour  and  eight  phone  calls  later,  Watkins  put  down  the  phone.  &quot;All  right,&quot;  he  said  to  his  wife.  &quot;Now  what?&quot;  &quot;I  don't  know,&quot;  she  said.  &quot;I  hon-  estly  can't  think.&quot;  Her  needlepoint  lay  in  her  lap,  and  she  picked  distractedly  at  the  arm  of  her  chair  with  the  needle.  &quot;'You've  called  everybody  I  can  think  of.&quot;  Watkins  was  silent  for  a  moment.  &quot;I  guess  there's  always  the  police,&quot;  he  said.  &quot;I  don't  see  the  point  in  that,&quot;  she  said.  &quot;It  isn't  as  though  he'd  been  kid-  napped,  or  anything.  We  know  why  he  went  away.&quot;  &quot;Possibly,&quot;  said  Watkins.  &quot;But  we  don't  know  what's  happened  since.&quot;  &quot;Still,&quot;  she  said,  and  then  she  was  silent.  &quot;As  a  matter  of  fact,  we  don't  know  why  he  left,&quot;  said  Watkins.  &quot;That  was  just  your  guess.&quot;  &quot;Well,  it's  a  pretty  good  guess,&quot;  she  said.  &quot;When  you  give  him  a  brutal  punishment,  and  he  doesn't  speak  again  except  to  say  he's  leaving  home,  it's  a  good  guess  that  the  punishment  was  re-  sponsible.&quot;  &quot;I  was  not  brutal,.  I  was  harsh-all  right,  maybe  a  little  too  harsh-but  I  was  not  brutal,  And  I  am  not  going  to  let  him  think  he  can  get  away  with  deliberate  lies.&quot;  &quot;If  you  were  too  harsh,  why  didn't  you  admit  it?&quot;&quot;  she  asked.  &quot;You're  not  going  to  accomplish  anything  if  you  behave  like  a  Prussian  drillmaster.&quot;  &quot;I  was  not  Prussian!&quot;  Watkins  snapped.  &quot;And  you  don't  accomplish  anything  if  you  give  a  child  a  punish-  ment  and  then  back  down.  You've  got  to  stick  to  what  you've  done.&quot;  &quot;A  little  understanding  is  a  great  deal  better  than  a  lot  of  ranting  and  shouting,  too.  I  wish  you'd  realize  that.&quot;  &quot;A  little  understanding?&quot;  Watkins  said,  his  voice  rising.  &quot;What  do  you  mean,  a  little  understanding?&quot;  &quot;If  you  knew  your  child  a  little  bet-  ter,  you'd  know  what  I  mean,&quot;  she  said.  &quot;You're  away  all  day.  You  see  him  maybe  a  total  of  three  hours  be-  tween  one  weekend  and  the  next.  And  then  you  come  in  and  stamp  around  and  shout  at  him.  I'm»  the  one  who  sees  him  all  day.  I'm  the  one  who  knows  what  he's  thinking.  But  doyou  ask   me  anything  before  you  punish  him?  You  do  not.  You  just  come  in  like  God  Almighty  and  crack  the  whip  and-&quot;  &quot;Wait  a  minute,&quot;  Watkins  said.  &quot;Wait  a  minute.  I  don't  see  as  much  of  him  as  you  do-granted.  There's  also  the  chance  that  that  makes  me  a  little  more  objective,  and  I  can  see  where  he  needs  more  discipline.&quot;  &quot;I  just  want  to  point  out  that  you  don't  know  everything,  and  that  I  know  him  a  little  better  than  you  do.&quot;  &quot;You  know  him  so  well  you  let  him  run  away,  and  you  calmly  announce  that  he'll  be  back  by  nightf43616e616461;  said  Watkins.  He  looked  at  his  watch.  &quot;It  is  now  twenty-three  minutes  past  nine,  and  I  submit  that  you  don't  know  him  as  well  as  you  think  you  do.&quot;  &quot;Would  you  have  had  any  better  ideas?&quot;  she  asked.  &quot;You  said  yourself  you  thought  of  having  him  wait  until  I  got  home,&quot;  Watkins  said.  &quot;You  could  have  done  that,  and  let  me  talk  with  him.&quot;  &quot;'You've  already  admitted  I  was  rightto  let  him  go,&quot;  she  said.  &quot;Let's  not  have  any  second-guessing,  if  it's  all  the  same  to  you.&quot;  &quot;I'm  not  second-guessing,&quot;  Watkins  said.  &quot;When  I  said  you  were  right,  I  was  simply  trying  to  be  agreeable.&quot;  &quot;Ha!&quot;  She  picked  up  her  needle-  point  and  began  to  sew  furiously.  &quot;If  you'd  be  a  little  more  agreeable  with  your  son,  these  problemswouldn't  come  up.&quot;  &quot;What  the  hell  are  you  talking  about?&quot;  he  shouted.  &quot;How  can  you  discipline  achild  and  be  agreeable?  Or  maybe  you  think  it's  all  right  for  him  to  get  away  with  these  things.&quot;  &quot;Now  you're  just  being  stupid,&quot;  she  said,  without  looking  up.  Watkins  went  to  the  front  hall,  flung  open  the  door  ofthecoat  closet,  and  reached  for  his  hat.  &quot;All  right,&quot;  he  said.  &quot;That  does  it.&quot;  He  jammed  his  hat  on  his  head  and  went  to  the  front  door.  &quot;What  does  what?&quot;  his  wife  asked.  &quot;Where  do  you  think  you're  going?&quot;  &quot;I'm  going  to  get  a  little  air,&quot;  said  Watkins.  He  opened  the  front  door.  &quot;I  am  also  going  to  look  for  our  son,&quot;  he  said,  and  went  out  and  slammed  the  door.  N  he  drove  through  the  dimly  light-  ed  streets,  Watkins  thought  back  to  the  night  before.  He  had  lost  his  temper,  which  he  obviously  shouldn't  have  done,  but  Donald  had  goaded  him  to  the  point  where  it  was  almost  un-  avoidable.  It  all  started  when  Watkins  got  home  and,  putting  the  car  in  the   garage,  noticed  that  one  of  the  windows  there  was  broken.  &quot;How  did  that  happen?&quot;  he  asked  his  wife.  &quot;I  don't  know,&quot;  she  said.  &quot;Donald  and  some  of  the  boys  were  playing  ball  this  afternoon.  Maybe  they  did  it.&quot;  Watkins  examined  the  window  and  then  saw  that  all  the  broken  glass  had  been  carefully  picked  up  and  disposed  of,  and  that  a  shingle  had  been  propped  against  the  inside  of  the  frame  in  an  at-  tempt  to  hide  the  hole.  He  went  into  the  house,  and  followed  the  sounds  of  gunfire  to  the  television  set,  where  Donald  was  sitting  with  a  soft  drink  in  one  hand  and  a  baseball  glove  on  the  other.  He  had  the  build  of  an  older  boy,  but  his  face  was  still  soft  and  young.  &quot;Who  broke  the  window?&quot;  Watkins  asked.  &quot;What  window?&quot;  said  Donald,  not  taking  his  eyes  off  the  screen.  &quot;The  garage  window,&quot;  Watkins  |  said.  &quot;I  don't  know,&quot;  Donald  replied.  &quot;Were  you  playing  ball  out  there  today?&quot;  (‘Nope.’)  Watkins  went  over  and  turned  off  the  television  set.  &quot;Listen,&quot;  he  said.  &quot;Your  mother  told  me  you  had  some  friends  here  today  and  you  were  play-  ing  ball.  Is  that  right?&quot;  &quot;Oh,  that,&quot;  said  Donald.  &quot;Yes.&quot;  &quot;Then  why  did  you  say  you  weren't?&quot;  &quot;I  didn't  understand  what  you  meant.&quot;  &quot;Who  broke  the  window?&quot;  &quot;I  don't  know.&quot;  &quot;Did  you  break  it?&quot;  (‘N0.),  &quot;Did  one  of  your  friends  break  it?&quot;  (‘NO.’)  &quot;Who  picked  up  the  glass?&quot;  &quot;I  don't  know.&quot;  &quot;Let  me  see  your  hands.&quot;  Watkins  took  the  soft-drink  bottle  and  the  glove  away  from  the  boy  and  examined  his  hands.  They  were  dirty,  but  otherwise  unmarked,  &quot;You're  sure  you  don't  know  who  did  it?&quot;  Watkins  asked.  (‘Nope.,7  Louise  came  into  the  room,  with  a  baseball  in  her  hand.  &quot;Is  this  yours?&quot;  she  asked,  holding  it  out  to  Donald.  He  said  nothing.  &quot;Where  did  you  find  it?&quot;  Watkins  asked.  &quot;In  the  garage,&quot;  she  said.  Watkins  took  the  ball,  and  looked  at  Donald.  &quot;All  right,&quot;  he  said.  &quot;Why  did  you  lie  to  me?&quot;  Donald  shrugged.  &quot;An-  swer  me!&quot;  Watkins  said,  his  voice  ris-  ing,  and  Donald  shrugged  again.  &quot;Go                                               up  to  your  room!&quot;  W  atkins  sho  ut  ed,  and  Donal  d  got  up  and  walked  s  lowl  y  away  and  upstairs. The  b  oy  was  sitting  on  the  edge  of  the  bed,  his  face  expr  essio  nles  s.  &quot;All  right,&quot;  said  Wa  tki  ns.  &quot;We'r  e  g  oi  n  g  to  put  an  en  d  to  this  l  ying  r  ig  h  t  now. He  turned  around  and  stamped  out  of  the  room,  t  rem  b  lin  g.  The  last  thi  ng  he  remembered  seei  ng  was  the  shocked  disbel  ief  in  Donald's  ey  es.  WATKINS  stopped  the  car  in  front  of  the  town's  main  drugstore,  and  went  inside.  Three  of  Louise's  friends  were  sitting  in  a  booth,  their  chatter  punctuated  by  the  loud  sucking  of  soda  straws  against  the  bottoms  of  glasses.  Watkins  thought  of  asking  them  if  they  had  seen  Donald,  but  something  made  him  reluctant  to  do  so;  he  had  the  feel-  ing  they  would  overdramatize  the  thing,  or,  worse,  sneer  at  him  behind  his  back.  The  boy  tending  the  soda  fountain  saw  him,  and  said,  &quot;Evening,  Mr.  Watkins.  What  can  I  do  for  you?&quot;  Watkins  hesitated,  then  walked  to  the  fountain  and  sat  on  one  of  the  stools.  &quot;Give  me  a  small  Coke,  please,&quot;  he  said,  and  when  the  boy  put  the  glass  in  front  of  him  he  asked,  as  casually  as  possible,  &quot;You  haven't  seen  Donald  around  re-  cently,  have  you?&quot;  &quot;No,  sir,  I  haven't,&quot;  said  the  boy.  &quot;Hey,  girls!&quot;  he  called  to  the  booth.  &quot;Never  mind,&quot;  Watkins  said  sharp-  ly,  but  it  was  too  late.  &quot;Anybody  seen  Donald  Watkins?&quot;  the  boy  asked.  &quot;Why?&quot;  one  of  the  girls  said.  &quot;Is  he  lost?&quot;  &quot;Mr.  Watkins,  here,  wants  to  know,&quot;  the  boy  said.  Never  mind,&quot;  Watkins  said  again,  more  quietly,  ©Yeeks!&quot;  said  another  girl.  &quot;A  kid-  napping!&quot;  &quot;When  was  he  lost,  Mr.  Watkins?&quot;  another  one  asked.  &quot;He  isn't  lost,  exactly,&quot;  Watkins   said,  and  hurriedly  finished  his  drink.  &quot;He  just  hasn't  come  home  yet,  that's  all.&quot;  &quot;Maybe  he  went  to  the  movie,&quot;  said  the  first  girl.  &quot;'To  see  'Marty'?  Are  you  déran-  gée?&quot;  said  the  third.  &quot;Maybe  heran  away  from  home,  then,&quot;  the  first  said.  Watkins  put  aquarter  on  the  count-  er,  and  got  off  the  stool.  &quot;As  a  matter  of  fact,  he's  probably  home  by  now,&quot;  he  said.  &quot;I  just  wondered  if  anybody  had  seen  him.&quot;  He  said  good  night,  and  went  out  and  got  in  his  car.  Just  on  a  chance,  he  stopped  in  front  of  the  movie  theatre,  but  the  box  office  was  closed,  and  the  doorman  said  he  couldn't  remember  seeing  anybody  of  Donald's  description.  Watkins  turned  away,  and  then  he  decided  to  call  his  wife.  It  was  just  possible  that  Donald  had  gone  home,  he  thought,  although  at  the  same  time  he  knew  that  he  hadn't.  The  doorman  let  Watkins  into  the  lobby,  where  there  was  a  payphone,  and  as  he  waited  for  his  wife  to  an-  swer  his  ring,  he  tried  to  compose  a  quick  prayer.  'The  minute  he  heard  her  frantic  &quot;Hello!,&quot;  he  knew  the  boy  was  not  home.  &quot;I  just  called  to  check,&quot;  he  said.  &quot;Where  are  you?&quot;  his  wife  asked.  He  told  her  where  he  was  and  where  he'd  been,  and  said,&quot;T'll  drive  around  a  little  more,  and  then  I'll  be  back.&quot;  &quot;All  right,&quot;  she  said  dully,  and  hung  up.  He  got  into  his  car,  and  as  he  drove  slowly  around  the  town  he  realized  that  the  sensation  he  was  experiencing  was  similar  to  one  he  had  had  before.  He  tried  to  remember  when  it  was,  and  eventually  it  came  to  him;  he  had  felt  this  strange  unreality,  this  sense  of  wait-  ing  for  something  that  would  never  hap-  pen,  during  the  twenty-two  hours  be-  fore  Louise  was  born.  He  had  stayed  at  the  hospital  for  a  while,  and  then  gone  home  and  slept,  hoping  to  make  the  time  pass.  But  he  had  wakened  after  only  an  hour,  wondering  if  it  was  still  the  same  day.  Now  there  was  an  identical  sensation,  but  with  the  difference  that  time  had  speeded  up,  not  slowed  down;  with  each  quarter  hour,  he  became  more  certain  that  something  bad  had  hap-  pened.  He  looked  at  his  watch;  it  showed  ten  minutes  to  eleven,  and  it  seemed  a  very  short  time  ago  that  he  had  been  predicting  Donald's  return  by  dinner.  He  looked  at  his  watch  again,  a  lit-  tle  later,  and  now  it  was  after  eleven,  and  he  knew  that  something  had  hap-  pened-either  that,  or  Donald  had  left  home  really  meaning  never  to  come    back.  In  whichever  case,  it  was  Watkins'  fault,  and  he  alone  would  have  to  bear  the  blame.  His  wife  was  not  in  the  living  room  when  he  got  home,  and  he  called  her  and  got  a  murmured  answer  from  up-  stairs.  He  went  up  to  their  bedroom,  and  found  her  sitting  in  a  chair  and  staring  out  the  window.  &quot;Going  to  bed?&quot;  Wat-  kins  asked.  &quot;No,&quot;  she  said,  still  looking  out  the  window.  She  lit  a  cigarette,  inhaled  deeply,  and  put  the  match  in  a  cluttered  ashtray  on  the  window  sill.  &quot;I  went  out  for  a  while  and  looked  around,&quot;  she  said.  &quot;&quot;Then  I  thought  somebody  had  better  stay  here,  just  in  case.&quot;  She  in-  haled  again,  and  stamped  the  cigarette  out  in  the  ashtray.  Watkins  went  over  and  crouched  down  beside  her  chair.  &quot;Listen  to  me,&quot;  he  said.  &quot;I  know  this  doesn't  do  much  good,  but  I'm  sorry.  I'm  sorry  I  shouted  at  you,  I'm  sorry  for  what  I  did  to  Donald,  and  I  wish  to  God  there  were  something  more  I  could  do  than  just  say  I'm  sorry.  It's  all  my  fault,  and  any-  thing  you  want  to  say  about  me  is  true.  I  mean  that.&quot;  She  looked  at  him,  and  almost  smiled.  &quot;Don't  be  silly,&quot;  she  said.  &quot;It's  no  more  your  fault  than  it  is  mine.  We  both  managed  to  do  it  wrong,  but  one  of  us  is  no  more  to  blame  than  the  other.&quot;  &quot;I  still  had  no  cause  to  shout  at  you,&quot;  he  said.  &quot;You,  of  all  people.&quot;  &quot;We  were  bothnervous,  but  we  were  afraid  to  show  it,  that's  [REDACTED];  she  said.  &quot;I  just  wanted  to  get  it  on  the  rec-  ord,&quot;  Watkins  said.  &quot;Please  forgive  me.&quot;  This  time  she  did  smile.  &quot;You're  for-  given,&quot;  she  said  quietly.  He  looked  slowly  around  the  room,  as  though  searching  for  something.  &quot;I  suppose  we  ought  to  call  the  police,&quot;  he  said,  at  last.  &quot;I  guess  so.&quot;  He  went  to  the  phone  between  the  beds,  dialled  the  operator,  and  asked  forthe  police  station.  After  three  rings,  a  voice  said,  &quot;Sergeant  Halley.&quot;  &quot;'This  is  Herbert  Watkins  calling,&quot;  Watkins  said,  keeping  his  voice  as  steady  and  his  words  as  precise  as  he  could.  &quot;I'd  like  to  report  a  missing  child.&quot;  &quot;O.K.,  Mr.  Watkins,&quot;  said  the  Ser-  geant.  &quot;Who  is  it?&quot;  &quot;It's  my  son,  Donald,&quot;  said  Wat-  kins.  &quot;Age  nine,  height&quot;-he  covered  the  mouthpiece  and  looked  at  his  wife,  who  made  an  estimate  with  her  hand-  |  &quot;height  about  four  feet  six,  brown  hair,  blue  eyes,  weight  I  don't  know-seven-  ty,  eighty-five  .  ..  I  really  don't-&quot;  &quot;Ninety-three,&quot;  his  wife  said.  &quot;Weight  ninety-three  pounds,&quot;  Watkins  said.  &quot;What  was  he  wearing?&quot;  the  Ser-  geant  asked.  &quot;Just  a  minute,&quot;  he  asked  his  wife,  &quot;A  red-and-white  T  shirt  and  blue  jeans,&quot;  she  said.  &quot;And  sneakers,&quot;  she  added,  as  an  afterthought.  Watkins  relayed  this  to  the  Sergeant.  &quot;Uh-huh,&quot;  said  the  Sergeant.  &quot;And  when  did  you  last  see  him?&quot;  &quot;A  little  after  noon  today.&quot;  &quot;Uh-huh.  Did  he  say  where  he  was  going?&quot;  &quot;Yes,&quot;  said  Watkins,  and  cleared  his  throat.  &quot;He  said  he  was  running  away  from  home.&quot;  There  was  a  pause,  and  then  the  Sergeant  said,  &quot;O.K.,  Mr.  Watkins.  I'll  put  this  on  the  radio  now,  and  we'll  have  all  the  cars  on  the  lookout.  If  we  don't  find  him  by  morning,  we'll  get  out  the  dogs.&quot;  &quot;Thank  you,&quot;  said  Watkins,  and  hung  up.  He  walked  across  to  his  wife.  &quot;I  think  Pl  go  downstairs  and  read  for  awhile,&quot;  he  said.  &quot;Is  there  anything  I  can  get  you?&quot;  &quot;No,  thanks,&quot;  she  said.  &quot;Not  right  now,.&quot;  said  Watkins,  and  HE  had  been  reading  for  perhaps  half  an  hour,  trying  to  concentrate  on  the  words,  which  seemed  to  recede  into  thepages,  when  he  heard  a  slight  noise  outside  the  frontdoor.  He  leaped  from  his  chair  and  ran  to  the  door  and  opened  it,  and  Louise  and  Pin-Head  Gerlack  sprang  apart  as  though  there  had  been  an  explosion  between  them.  Pin-Head  put  one  hand  to  his  mouth,  and  Louise  looked  angrily  at  Watkins.  &quot;Oh-I'm  sorry,&quot;  Watkins  said.  &quot;I  thought  it  might  be  Donald.&quot;  &quot;And  what,  may  I  ask,  would  I  be  doing  outhere  with  Donald?&quot;  Louise  said  coldly.  Watkins  looked  into  the  night  beyond  her,  and  saw  that  it  had  begun  to  rain.  &quot;Nothing,&quot;  he  said.  &quot;It's  just  that  he  hasn't  come  in  yet.&quot;  &quot;W  hat?&quot;  she  said.  pened? Watkins  turned  the  heat  off  under  the  coffee  and  ran  out  of  the  house  and  to  his  car,  and  in  less  than  a  minute  he  was  speed-  ing  over  the  slippery  roads.  He  didn't  think  or  hope;  he  just  drove  as  fast  as  possible.  He  parked  the  car  as  close  to  the  woods  as  he  could,  then  ran  across  the  intervening  field.  The  wet  grass  made  his  legs  cold  and  clammy  up  to  the  knees,  and  he  stepped  in  some  boggy  ground  that  filled  his  shoes  with  water,  but  he  kept  on  running  until  he  reached  the  edge  of  the  woods,  and  then  he  slowed  down  and,  panting,  searched  through  the  underbrush  trying  tofind  some  sign  of  the  hut.  Finally,  he  came  upon  it,  in  a  small  clearing.  The  first  thing  he  saw,  after  the  hut  itself,  was  Donald's  suitcase  in  the  entrance,  and  then,  farther  inside,  he  saw  the  boy's  feet,  protruding  from  un-  der  a  dirty  blanket.  Watkins  leaned  against  a  tree  and  waited  until  his  heart-  beat  became  more  nearly  normal;  then  he  got  down  on  his  hands  and  knees,  and  crawled  into  the  hut.  Donald  woke  up,  and  looked  at  him.  &quot;Hi,&quot;  said  Watkins,  and  smiled.  &quot;Sleep  well?&quot;  &quot;Hi,&quot;  Donald  said.  He  yawned,  and  stretched,  and  then  he,  too,  smiled.  &quot;Not  so  very  well,&quot;  he  said.  &quot;Neither  did  I,&quot;  said  Watkins.  &quot;Let's  go  get  some  breakfast.&quot;  Donald  stretched  again,  then  rolled  out  of  the  blanket  and  threw  it  in  a  corner.  Watkins  backed  out  of  the  hut  and  stood  up,  and  the  boy  joined  him,  bringing  his  suitcase  without  a  word.  He's  won,  Watkins  thought  as  they  started  across  the  wet  field  together.  He's  won,  all  right,  but  he  must  never  know  how  big  his  victory  is.  Never  in  a  hundred  years.  -NaTtHaniEt  BeEncnu®Ey  Albert  Da  Costa,  Met  tenor,  attempting  his  first  Siegmund,  gave  a  satisfactory  ac-  count  of  himself. N              PALL  MALL  CREAM  SHERRY  Imported  by  THE  PADDINGTON  CORP.,  630  Fifth  Ave.,  [REDACTED]  20,  [REDACTED]     114  O  N  A  N  D  O  F  F  T  H  E  A  V  E  N  U  E  O  anyone  who  takes  the  fash-  ion  forecasters  liter-  ally,  the  future  is  be-  Jn  Tor  dizened  indeed.  On  Et'i‘f.‘  all  sides,  there  are  hats  of  velvet  and  satin  and  brocade,  made  even  more  magnificent  with  trailing  ostrich  plumes,  furs,  feathers,  veiling,  flowers,  and  satin  bows.  Obviously  the  small  taxis  have  had  no  influence,  for  the  hats  tower  and  puff  and  tilt  at  a  precarious  pace.  These  extravaganzas  can  be  pret-  ty  marvellous  by  themselves.  But  we  are  also  told  that  our  swanlike  necks  should  be  swathed  in  pearlescent  beads  and  other  glitter  and  that  our  dresses  should  be  draped  up  to  roses  high  on  the  bosom,  in  the  manner  of  the  almost  for-  gotten  designer  Lucile.  It  is  suggested  that  we  wear  our  sables,  too.  Put  them  all  together  (let  us  pray  that  no  one  does),  and  we'll  have  achieved  the  romantic,  fallen-  woman  elegance  of  Sadie  Thompson.  Properly  handled,  though,  these  hats  can  accomplish  love-  ly  coquetry  or  moody  drama,  my  soon-to-be  fine-feathered  friends,  plus  a  lot  of  frolic  for  not  only  les  jeunes  but  les  not-s0-jeunes.  To  quote  Mr.  John,  a  master  hand  at  custom  millinery,  fashion  seems  to  go  in  cycles;  first,  the  head  and  hat  are  incon-  spicuous,  while  the  rest  of  the  costume  draws  the  crowds  (as  in  Dior's  43616e616461Look),  and  then  the  dress  is  played  down  (as  in  the  current  sheath)  and  the  hat  takes  the  bows.  I  don't  see,  however,  why  women  can't  be  at  both  ends  of  the  cycle  this  fall-on  different  days,  of  course.  The  luxurious  furred  and  feathered  new  hats  should  certainly  be  ac-  companied  by  the  most  basic  dress.  The  many  brocade  and  velvet  Ori-  ental  turbans  usually  have  one  smashing  jewel  on  them,  and  any  further           -&lt;     FEMININE  FASHIONS  ornamentation  flashier  than  a  single  strand  of  pearls  would  be  too  much.  But  there  are  also  hats  simple  to  the  point  of  starkness,  and  these  could  surmount  a  fairly  complicated  costume.  Some  are  throwbacks  to  theera  of  Dickensian  dandies.  Such  as  (1)  Sally  Victor's  black  beaver  with  a  wide,  rolled-up  brim,  a  tall,  squared  crown,  a  band  of  cocoa  satin,  and  a  tiny  curl  of  ostrich  at  one  side;  and  (2)  her  tilted  pale-beige  beaver  with  a  black  grosgrain  band  and  (at  one  side)  a  black  feather  wing  in  the  ascendant;  and  (3)  John  Frederics'  enchanting  Belle  Brummell,  with  an  ingénue  arched  cloche  brim  and  a  high  crown  slanting  backward.  Then,  there  are  tubes  like  those  Adolfo  makes  for  Emmé-the  base  curved  smoothly  along  the  hairline  and  the  rest  rising  high  above  it,  with  a  slight  flare  toward  the  top.  They're  as  stark  as  the  head-        &quot;Would  you  mind  letting  me  go  to  the  front  while  I'm  stull  seething?&quot;  gear  of  an  Egyptian  princess,  which  they  very  much  resemble.  And  there  are  all  sorts  of  unelaborate  fez  shapes.  And  there  are  Walter  Florell's  little  felt  Irene  Castle  hats,  which  are  likely  to  have  lofty,  square  crowns  and  to  de-  scend  without  a  perceptible  break  into  a  small  cloche  edge.  This  edge  is  caught  in  by  a  pleat  at  the  side  to  give  it  an  ir-  regular,  profile  look.  They  couldn't  be  simpler  or  younger.  The  cry  of  &quot;Who'd  wear  them?&quot;  that  was  raised  about  those  inverted  salad  bowls  of  last  spring  (most  of  them  were  rather  a  blot  on  the  landscape)  is  being  raised  about  the  elaborate  hats  of  this  autumn.  Well,  they're  more  be-  coming  to  more  women  than  you'd  think.  In  the  first  place,  our  milliners  have  rediscovered  the  profile  hat,  and  tilts  have  broken  the  monopoly  of  past  seasons'  sternly  balanced,  even-keel  pro-  ductions.  Sally  Victor  and  Lilly  Daché  are  in  the  front  rank  of  this  tendency.  What  is  more  important,  however,  is  that  even  the  fluffiest  and  most  mountainous  hats  are  clean-limbed  and  uncluttered  where  they  frame  the  face.  Small  bonnet  or  cloche  or  sailor  brims  may  be  piled  with  black  fox  (everybody  loves  Brer  Fox  this  year),  but  the  facing  of  the  brims  is  smooth  and  true  to  the  shape  of  the  head  and  face.  If  pillboxes  suit  you,  you  can  wear  them  towering  up  to  toque  height  or  swathed  in  layers  and  layers  of  tulle,  very  Maxim's,  but  the  line  next  to  your  hair  will  be  serene.  Turbans  and  toques,  no  matter  how  tall  or  intricately  draped,  often  start  with  a  neat,  clinging  cap  line  ora  small  roll,  either  tilted  or  straight,  on  the  forehead.  (The  milliners  have  tried  to  make  women  wear  hats  well  down  on  their  foreheads,  but  the  resistance  was  so  dogged  they've  given  up,  for  the  time  being.) UT  instead  of  skipping  about,  let  us  pay  more  leisurely  visits  to  some  of  the  custom  salons,  where  the  fun  and  the  news  are  the  spectaculars,  despite  the  presence  of  many  beautiful  simple  hats.  Sally  Victor,  who  wants  women  to  look  treasured,  has  got  together  a  par-  ticularly  dazzhng  collection  of  fur  masterpieces.  'There  is  blond  lynx  mak-  ing  a  fluffy  band  around  the  wide  crown  of  a  white  felt  sailor-cloche,  which  is  just  as  terrific  when  her  scheme  is  black  fox  and  white  satin.  Lynx,  too,  makes  a  skyscraping  dome  above  a  straight  and  narrow  brim  of  white  ribbed  silk,  and  a  white  feather  wing  shoots  up  one  side.  (Vlctor  puts  sharp  wings,  some  as  much  as  six  inches  long,  on  almost  everything,  and  she  uses  satin  rosettes  to  indent  fur  hats  and  puffy  hats.)  A  small  feltroll  that  goes  around  the  hairline  is  some-  times  topped  by  mink  draped  into  a  sidewise  toque,  with  some  of  those  rosettes  going  up  the  back;  another  draped  one  is  of  beige  556e6974656420537461746573n  broad-  tail.  As  for  tiny  hats,  she  shows  minute  derbies,  either  of  the  same  broadtail,  with  a  black  grosgrain  band  and  a  rosette,  or  of  black  velvet  with  a  white  swansdown  crown.  Minx,  that's  what  they  are-pure  minx.  As  for  feathers,  she  is  partial  to  spiky  ones  in  a  variety  of  colors.  ('There  are  lots  of  feathers  everywhere,  as  I've  said,  but  all  that  most  milliners  know  about  them  is  that  they  are  from  the  birds;  hardly  anyone  can  tell  you  what  genus  is  involved.)  Feathers  make  a  purple  boa  around  the  crown  of  a  purple  beaver  sailor  and-  this  is  sheer  Gainsborough-a  black  puffy  hunk  atop  the  high  side  of  an  enormous,  tilted  white  beaver  picture  hat.  Like  many  others,  Victor  loves  filmy  restaurant  hats  of  nylon  tulle  and  malines  for  grand  entrances  into  the  various  Rectors  of  today.  Hers  are  usu-  ally  in  mysterious,  who-is-she?  black  (tilted  caps  swathed  with  black  dotted  net  and  sprouting  black  wing  décor,  toques  wrapped  in  tulle  with  rhinestone  crescents  catching  the  stuff  in  here  and  there)  or  blond  (something  of  cocoa  nylon  malines  with  tiny  matching  wisps  of  feather  spraying  out  at  intervals).  Her  pet  late-day  hat,  however,  is  a  tulle  cap  with  a  huge  Norman  bow  across  the  top  of  the  head  and  a  veil  brim  edged  with  black  lace-the  kind  of  thing  that  makes  fathomless  pools  of  your  eyes,  And  there  are  quantities  of  hats  with  small  bonnet  brims  and  soaring,  lopsided  toque  drapery  above. AND  16  CONVENIENT  BRANCHES  *  Trademark        shown  in  gold  satin,  is  smothered  with  polka-dot  black  net  veiling;  another,  thoroughly  Victorian,  is  of  magenta  velvet-striped  silk,  with  a  chin  strap  and  roses  up  the  side.  TURBANS,  and  toques  draped  in  turban  fashion,  have  always  been  the  specialty  of  Lilly  Daché.  (Virtually  everything  of  hers  tilts  in  the  profile  manner  beloved  by  Frenchwomen  the  world  over.)  There  are  real  potentate  turbans,  wide  and  fat  and  wrapped  diag-  onally  upward,  often  of  brilliant  metal-  lic  brocades  that  are  perversely  veiled  by  smoky  malines  to  tone  down  the  bril-  liance.  Many  other  turbans,  tall  but  drooping  to  one  side,  are  of  satin  in  Dache's  ingenious  color  combinations-  lime,  Kelly  green,  and  mauve,  for  in-  stance.  A  fluff  of  burnt  ostrich  tops  off  a  tiny  horsehair  pillbox  to  be  worn  either  back  over  the  chignon  or  down  on  the  forehead;  &quot;aigrettes&quot;  of  the  ostrich  pop  up  the  side  of  a  tiny  evening  derby  of  black  net  with  a  velvet  brim  or  burgeon  from  a  rhinestone  ornament  that  flanks  a  deep  pillbox  of  black  malines.  Daché  is  also  given  to  those  piled-up,  draped,  tilted  toque  crowns,  mostly  springing  up  from  a  smooth  line  that  slants  across  the  forehead,  Gold  sequins  and  braid  shimmer  through  the  top  two  of  the  many  layers  of  brown  malines  that  crown  a  527573736961n  sort  of  toque;  red  se-  quins  dot  one  that  is  all  horizontal  folds  of  black  net.  Another  Dache  conceit  is  flowers  pressed  under  filmy  fabrics;  a  toque  of  Shocking  satin  is  covered  with  pressed  roses  of  many  hues  under  a  blanket  of  green  veiling,  and  there  are  pressed  apricot-colored  flowers  under  the  brown  tulle  that  swathes  a  fat,  squat  little  pillbox.  More  roses,  in  a  vast  vari-  ety  of  colors,  are  piled  on  the  tiny  brim  of  another  delight,  and  a  single  rose  bobbles  at  the  top  of  it.  My  own  par-  ticular  love,  which  has  no  flowers  at  all,  is  a  tailored  job  built  entirely  of  taupe  satin.  A  little  brim  curls  up  at  one  side,  the  crown  is  irregularly  draped  and  square,  and  taupe  malines  veiling  goes  over  the  whole  thing.  Modern  Edward  VIIs  would  love  the  mock  demureness  of  this.  Then  comes  a  melding  of  fur  and  flower-a  chéchia  of  red  Ameri-  can  broadtail  with  a  red  rose  crushing  the  summit  down  to  one  side.  Watteau  is  the  background  of  a  hat  of  black  or  white  ostrich-a  flat  plaque  on  the  forehead  and  three  curly  plumes  at  the  top  of  the  head.  This  one  can  be  reversed,  so  that  the  plumes  make  a  tiara  in  front. LL  the  crowns  at  John  Frederics  are  high,  but  Mr.  Fred  likes  them  slanting  backward,  so  their  height  is  not  conspicuous  from  the  front.  This  is  true  of  his  frequent  turbans,  which  slope  astern  to  end  in  a  peaked  minaret.  These  wrap  upward  over  the  ears,  and  there's  a  flat  twist  above  the  widow's  peak.  A  favorite  color  blend  of  his  is  pale  turquoise  and  olive  green,  as  in  a  turban  of  turquoise  net  with  olive  velvet  twisted  through  the  folds.  Other  tur-  bans  are  in  tricolor  combinations-olive  velvet  next  to  the  face,  turquoise  satin  above,  and  a  peak  the  colorofthe  in-  side  of  a  ripe  olive,  or  else  beige  velvet  on  one  side,  cognac  on  the  other,  and  an  orange  tip.  You  can  also  get  beau-  ties  of  this  tilted-back-turban  variety  done  entirely  in  black  soleil,  and  smash-  ers  they  are.  Mr.  Fred  likes  fur  for  turbans,  too;  these  come  well  down  over  the  ears  and  recede  from  the  hairline  at  the  middle  ofthe  forehead.  One  of  raccoon  is  very  round  and  six  inches  deep;  another,  of  platinum  mink,  is  pulled  upward  into  a  draped  knot  in  front.  Realistic  feather  flowers  with  feathery  green  leaves  get  a  good  work-  out  from  him.  Wavy-brimmed  sailors  of  velvet  often  have  a  wreath  of  them  in  anemone  colors.  For  handsome  lunacy,  Mr.  Fred  gives  you  a  cap  with  white  ostrich  spilling  over  the  face  like  the  |;  hair  of  a  sheepdog,  and  ared  feather  rose  planted  high  at  one  side. DOWN  at  Walter  Florell's  house,  there  is  a  preoccupation  with  all  the  didoes  of  the  early  part  of  the  cen-  tury.  'Those  simple  Irene  Castle  felts  of  his  may  appear  in  gray  with  a  tiny  raised  stripe  that  suggests  Jimmy  Walk-  er's  pants.  Silk  mesh  motoring  veils  with  chiffon  edges  also  appear,  dec-  orating,  say,  a  deep  felt  pillbox  that  has  an  oversized  tortoise-shell  buckle  across  the  front.  Florell's  Roaring  Twenties  turbans  start  with  a  cap  made  of  kid-glove  kid  and  pile  up  from  that  simple  base.  He  has  always  liked  big  sailors  and  imposing  wide  berets.  A  cur-  rent  example  is  a  black  beaver  sailor  with  a  wide-bloused  alabaster  beaver  crown;  another  is  a  red  felt  beret  with  a  dou-  ble  edge  rippling  upward  over  each  eye.  Mousse  felt,  so  long-haired  that  he  calls  it  Tchaikovsky  (oh  dear!  ),  makes  oth-  er  berets,  some  folded  both  on  top  and  underneath,  and  caps  suggesting  the  Jackie  Coogan  of  &quot;The  Kid.&quot;  The  flamboyance  of  the  minstrel  show  lives  again  in  stovepipe  hats  whose  crowns  slant  backward.  'These  are  completely  covered  with  confetti-dot  sequins.  Though  Florell  names  his  ostrich  hats  after  [REDACTED]  Guinan,  they  are  among  the  most  youthful  and  demure  ones  aroun  sailor-clochesin  white  felt  orred  velvet  and  with  white  ostrich  spilling  over  the  brim.  And  his  small  white  ermine  beret  pillbox  is  tops.  It  has  a  black  ostrich  plume  starting  high  at  the  side  and  curling  down  to  caress  the  jaw  and  cup  the  chin.  The  Em-  press  Eugénie  should  have  lived  to  see  the  day. [REDACTED]  THE  NEW  43616e616461ER  ian  toque  starts  with  a  draped  pillbox  of  violet  velvet  that  is  long  front  to  back  but  barely  wide  enough  to  support  the  pile  of  roses  and  a  small  bird  shooting  up  the  front;  her  favorite  shako,  made  of  red  velvet  with  a  wide  band  of  irides-  cent  green  feathers,  is  slender  indeed.  This  one  has  a  green  silk  mesh  veil  of  the  type  that  women  early  in  the  cen-  tury  poked  their  soda  straws  through  and  is  big  enough  to  allow  a  modern  cigarette,  with  some  maneuvering.  Not  everythingIrene  does  is  tall,  though.  There  are  her  satin  turbans,  some  of  which  come  down  to  the  ears  and  go  wide  at  the  sides  into  fattish  rolls  that  are  caught  in  above  the  temples  with  rhinestone  crescents.  She  also  does  caps  pushed  so  far  back  from  the  hairline  that  they  are  just  about  invisible  in  front.  Ornaments  are  placed  high  at  their  side  back.  A  tiny  tricorne  of  white  satin  with  a  one-inch  cuff  has  white  mink  tails  des-  cending  from  it;  a  rose  is  perched  high  on  a  cap  of  black  velvet,  and  flat  white  feathers  come  down  from  it  to  nestle  along  one  cheekbone;  a  cap  of  white  ostrich  flaunts  a  curly  white  plume  out  to  one  side.  Irene  loves  to  play  with  fur,  too,  and  she  remains  faithful  to  upside-  down  salad  bowls.  One  is  of  fitch;  an-  other  (no  fur  in  this)  is  of  black  velvet  covered  with  guinea-hen  pads  in  white  and  charcoal-spatteredwhite.  A  tilted  527573736961n  chéchic  is  worked  out,  logically,  |&quot;  in  527573736961n  sable.  Something  called  homo  pastel  mink  makes  a  tilted  toque  turban  in  which  the  skins  are  worked  into  a  snail  coil  toward  the  top.  HE  manic  young  talent  called  William  J.  pays  no  attention  to  ostrich  but  makes  much  of  shaggy  natu-  ral  cog  feathers  and  spiky  white  hackle  feathers.  He  lets  hackle  feathers  curl  up  around  a  head-hugging  cap  that  looks  like  a  shattered  Breton.  Two  of  his  beloved  quills  shoot  outward  at  one  side  of  it.  But  his  real  razzle-dazzle  is  accomplished  with  stripped  peacock,  wispy  and  iridescent,  which  he  uses  for  shimmering  fright  wigs  of  monstrous  size  (only  a  woman  with  the  humor  of  Faye  Emerson  could  get  away  with  these),  as  well  as  for  wearable  medium-  size  ones,  which  are  sort  of  transparent  berets.  The  only  hat  in  the  house  that  is  to  be  worn  sedately  straight  on  the  coiffure  is  a  deep,  fat  cap  of  swansdown,  and  in  this  case  a  miniature  swan's  head  descends  the  front.  For  quieter  moods,  there  are  nice  draped  and  tilted  toques  on  bonnet  frames,  in  the  Victor-Daché  tradition,  some  of  them  with  beret  crowns  that  can  be  pulled  around  to  suit  your  state  of  mind. We  note  that  the  basis  for  the  claim  for  reimbursement  for  the  rental  of  the  dinner  jacket  is  paragraph  79  of  the  Standardized  Government  Travel  Regulations.  That  paragraph  reads  as  follows:  Other  expenses  -Miscellaneous  expenditures,  not  enumerated  herein,  when  necessarily  in-  curred  by  the  traveler  in  connection  with  the  transaction  of  official  business,  will  be  allowed  when  approved.  In  order  to  qualify  for  reimbursement  under  the  regulations,  just  quoted,  the  ex-  penditure,  in  addition  to  an  administrative  approval  thereof,  must  be  one  &quot;necessarily  incurred  by  the  traveler  in  connection  with  the  transaction  of  official  business.&quot;  Fur-  thermore,  paragraph  2  of  the  referred-to  regulations  provides  that  the  reimbursable  expenses  under  the  regulations  are  &quot;con-  fined  to  those  expenses  essential  to  the  transacting  of  the  official  business.&quot;  Doubtless  a  person  occupying  an  official  position  of  the  rank  here  involved  may  be  expected  to  receive  official  dinner  invita-  tions  from  time  to  time  during  official  travel  and  to  be  prepared  therefor  so  far  as  concerns  proper  dress  required  for  the  occasion.  The  invitation  card  attached  to  the  voucher  indicates  the  proper  dress  as  &quot;Dinner  Jacket  or  Uniform.&quot;  The  claim-  ant's  failure  to  take  with  him  necessary  clothing  to  meet  reasonably  anticipated  personal  necessities  is  not  considered  suffi-  cient  to  shift  the  burden  of  the  cost  of  procuring  such  clothing  from  personal  to  official  business.  In  the  circumstances,  the  rental  of  the  dinner  jacket  legally  may  not  be  deemed  to  be  essential  to  the  &quot;trans-  acting  of  the  official  business&quot;  within  con-  templation  of  those  words  as  used  in  the  regulations.  'The  voucher,  which  is  returned  here-  with,  may  not  be  certified  for  payment  so  far  as  concerns  the  item  covering  the  rental  of  the  dinner  jacket.  *  PO43616e616461~V  ACATION  PIBROCH  Only  last  Monday  We  sighted  Fundy;  Tuesday  began  so  Blithely  at  Canso;  Wednesday  we  jotted  down  Gaelic  at  Englishtown;  Thursday  we  met,  on  Hilly  Cape  Breton,  Bagpipers,  tall  and  thin,  Looking  like  Errol  Flynn. THE  BARBIZON  CORPORATION  8     475  Fifth  [REDACTED],  [REDACTED]  17,  N.  w     Y.  124  OUR  F  AR~FLUNG  THERE  IS  INDEED  IA43616e616461  spring,  when  my  wife  and  I  were  getting  ready  for  an  ex-  tended  motor  trip  through  537061696e,  one  of  the  places  we  decided  we  wanted  to  see  was  the  small  city  of  Ronda.  We  were  put  on  to  it  by  Richard  Ford's  &quot;Handbook  for  Travellers  in  537061696e.&quot;  There  isn't  much  to  say  about  Richard  Ford  except  that  he  was  one  of  those  eighteenth,  and  early-nineteenth-cen-  tury  Englishmen  who  seem  to  have  had  it  pretty  much  their  own  way.  A  bar-  rister  who  never  practiced,  he  collected  majolica,  built  up  a  fine  gallery  of  pic-  tures,  contributed  at  his  leisure  to  the  Quarterly  Review,  and  did  a  lot  of  get-  ting  around.  From  1830  through  1833,  he  was  in  537061696e,  travelling  the  country  on  horseback,  and  in  1840,  at  the  in-  vitation  of  the  [REDACTED]  publisher  John  Murray,  who  was  bringing  outa  series  of  guidebooks  to  the  various  European  countries,  he  began  compiling  his  &quot;Handbook.&quot;  Published  in  1845,  it  ran  through  six  editions  but  has  now  long  been  out  of  print.  I  was  lucky  enough  to  come  across  a  copy,  however,  and  no  sooner  had  I  looked  into  it  than  I  knew  I  was  in  the  company  of  a  man  as  knowledgeable  and  entertaining  as  &quot;Let  me  ask  you  this.  In  the  game  of  Farmer  in  the  Dell,  for  immstance,  were  you  frequently  the  cheese  that  stood  alone?&quot;  BUT  ONE  RONDA  his  better-known  fellow-Englishman  and  contemporary  George  Borrow.  Along  with  being  a  guidebook,  Ford's  work  is  a  collection  of  essays  on  various  aspects  of  537061696e,  the  Spanish,  and  their  life-cigars,  bullfights,  robbers,  smug-  glers,  inns,  cookery,  [REDACTED]  Theresa  of  Avila,  Don  Quixote,  and  so  on.  Page  after  page  struck  me  as  being  as  good  as  anything  in  Borrow's  &quot;'The  Bible  in  537061696e,&quot;  and  some  sections  as  even  better,  so  when  Ford  gave  it  as  his  opinion  that  Ronda  was  undoubtedly  one  of  the  most  picturesque  places  in  Europe,  adding,  'There  is  indeed  but  one  Ronda  in  the  world,&quot;  I  needed  no  furtherurging.  Neither  did  my  wifes  We  found  Ronda  on  our  road  map  and  circled  it  in  red.  The  circle  fell  across  some  of  the  highest  mountains  in  southern  537061696e.  Ronda,  which  lies  southeast  of  Seville  and  almost  due  west  of  Mélaga,  began  to  look  like  a  rather  difficult  place  to  get  to.  We  had  been  given  all  kinds  of  warnings  about  the  sorry  shape  of  some  Spanish  roads,  and  our  map  tended  to  confirm  them.  As  far  as  we  could  make  out  from  its  legend,  all  the  roads  that  went  to  Ronda  were  either  poor  or  bad  or  very  bad,  with  the  bad  and     SEPTEMBER  8,  19  5  6  the  very  bad  predominating.  Neverthe-  less,  since  we  had  intended  to  drive  from  Seville  to  Malaga  anyhow,  we  decided  to  take  our  chances  and  make  a  detour  by  way  of  Ronda.  The  fact  that  it  ap-  peared  to  be  more  accessible  by  burro  than  by  car  made  it  seem  all  the  more  interesting  as  a  port  of  call.  UR  automobile  was  a  little  French  one.  We  picked  it  up  in  [REDACTED]  and  drove  south  from  there.  Crossing  the  Spanish  border  at  Irun,  we  proceeded  to  Bilbao,  then  to  Burgos,  Segovia,  and  537061696e,  and  from  537061696e  to  Seville,  by  way  of  Oropesa,  Mérida,  and  a  number  of  other  small  towns  and  villages.  Though  by  the  standards  of  most  556e6974656420537461746573n  drivers  it  isn't  a  particularly  long  journey  from  4972616e  to  Seville-just  over  five  hundred  miles  by  the  most  di-  rect  route-it  took  us  nearly  a  month  to  make  the  trip,  but  then  we  didn't  go  by  anything  like  the  most  direct  route,  and  we  didn't  hurry.  Neither  my  wife  nor  I  had  ever  been  in  537061696e  before,  and  there  was  so  much  to  do  and  see,  so  much  won-  der  and  excitement,  that  some  days  on  the  road  we  wouldn't  make  fifty  miles.  Three  things,  by  the  time  we  reached  Seville,  I  had  made  up  my  mind  about:  that  537061696e,  scenically,  is  the  most  aston-  ishing  country  in  Europe;  that  the  Spaniards  are  among  the  kindliest  of  all  the  world's  peoples;  and  that  after  some  of  the  roads  we  had  travelled,  carrying  us  up,  down,  and  across  mountains,  andaround  hairpin  curves  in  dizzying  de-  scents  (one  day  we  took  a  wrong  turning  and  rode  for  miles  and  miles  across  a  high,  bare,  desolate  moor  with  the  snow-capped  peaks  of  a  sierrarising  alongside  us  less  than  a  mile  away  )-after  such  an  apprenticeship  we  had  no  reason  to  be  in-  timidated  by  what  any  map  said  about  the  roads  to  Ronda.  Not  much  larger  than  a  burro,  our  little  French  car  had  proved  itself  just  as  de-  pendable.  I  felt  that  it  and  we  made  a  good  team.  There  was  hardly  a  place  in  537061696e  that  I  didn't  hate  to  leave,  and  I  especially  hated  THE  NEW  43616e616461ER  to  leave  Seville.  It  is  the  one  city  I  know  of  that  lives  up  absolutely  to  the  advance  billing  of  its  most  enthusiastic  admirers.  The  orange  trees  were  all  in  bloom  when  we  arrived,  and  many  other  flow-  ers,  too-carnations  and  oleanders  and  geraniums  and  a  greater  profusion  of  roses  than  I  have  ever  seen  anywhere  else.  Mornings  when  I'd  wake  to  the  sound  of  horses  and  hoofs  and  creaking  wheels,  for  Seville  is  still  in  large  part  a  horse-drawn  city,  and  perhaps  ofa  boy  singing  a  flamenco  song  on  his  way  to  work  (he  passed  our  corner  just  after  eight  o'clock  and  almost  invariably  he  was  singing)-on  such  mornings  I  wanted  to  stay  forever.  Ronda  didn't  interest  me  nearly  as  much  as  it  had  back  in  the  United  [REDACTED].  While  Ford  might  be  right  in  saying  that  there  was  but  one  Ronda  in  the  world,  so  was  there  but  one  Seville.  Why  should  I  want  to  go  anywhere  else?  When  we  started  driving  again,  how-  ever,  things  immediately  began  to  brighten.  What  happens  when  you  take  to  the  road  in  537061696e-especially  when  you  leave  the  highways-is  that  you  travel  a  trick  of  time,  one  that  curves  back  several  hundred  years.  'These  shepherds  and  goatherds,  this  goosegirl,  this  boy  driving  a  herd  of  swine,  this  other  boy  tending  a  flock  of  turkeys,  this  green  gypsy  cart  with  the  women  sitting  relaxed  in  their  beads  and  colored  garments  while  the  men  keep  pacewith  the  horse,  these  mountain  children  who  pour  out  of  their  huts  to  see  if  they  can  run  as  fast  as  your  slow-moving  car,  so  strange  is  the  sight  of  an  automobile  to  them-where  are  we,  and  in  what  age,  and  what  has  happened  to  the  centuries  in  between?  And  then,  alongwith  the  ever-  changing  Spanish  landscape-the  green  valleys,  the  aching  desolation  of  high  fastnesses  where  nothing  grows,  the  ruined  castles,  the  forgotten  villages  that  look  like  heaped-up  chunks  of  adobe-colored  earth,  the  chasms  and  gorges,  the  cork  forests,  the  olive  groves,  the  wild  flowers,  the  shock  of  surprise  on  surprise-along  with  this  face  of537061696e,thereare  the  faces  of  the  people  you  stop  totalk  with.  These  would  be  the  men,  mostly-farmers  and  shepherds  and  wagoners  and  la-  borers  on  the  roads-and  there  is  always  time  enough  to  take  time  out.  &quot;Com-  mand  me,&quot;  they  say.  &quot;W  hat  is  it  that  I  can  do?&quot;  Then,  when  you  inquire  about  a  distance  or  a  direction  or  a  place  to  stay,  there  is  a  sudden  collapse  of  the  severe  presence  with  which  the  Spaniard  confronts  the  world,  and  you  notice  the  quick,  warm  desire  to  establish  the  kind     125                  A  PERFECT  MATCH  ...  43616e616461ERLING  BY  GORHAM  as  SANDWICH  PLATE,  diam.  8&quot;,  $17.50;  9&quot;,  $26.50;  10&quot;,  $35. SQUIBB  ouvaurry  THE  PRICELESS  INGREDIENT        of  truly  human  communication  that  is  based  on  unfailing  courtesy  and  pride.  The  raggedest  vagrant  or  poorest  coun-  tryman  knows  that  he  is  your  equal  and  the  equal  of  any  man,  and,  in  his  own  secret  heart,  that  he  is  perhaps  a  little  better  than  most-a  Christian  gentle-  man,  an  old  and  wellborn  one  (cristiono  viejo  y  rancio)-and  he  conducts  him-  self  accordingly.  Whatever  the  offenses  or  iniquities  of  its  economic  structure-  and  they  are  many-537061696e  is  in  this  re-  spect  a  completely  classless  country.  To  offer  such  a  man  anything  in  the  way  of  money  would  be  as  unthinkable  as  it  would  be  for  him  to  accept  it.  A  cigarette,  however,  is  a  different  matter.  It  performs  the  same  service  as  the  breaking  of  bread.  In  the  country  regions,  the  cigarette  will  generally  be  once  refused  before  it  is  accepted,  in  keeping  with  the  ancient  canons  of  Spanish  correctness,  and  when  it  is  ac-  cepted,  you  smoke  and  chat  together,  and  even  your  atrocious  Spanish  is  for-  given.  &quot;'These  are  fine  goats  you  have.&quot;  &quot;They  will  do.&quot;  &quot;How  many  are  there?&quot;  &quot;A  hundred.&quot;  &quot;They  are  fine  animals.  But  tell  me-are  we  far  from  the  road  that  leads  to  Segovia?&quot;  &quot;No,  not  far.  Continue  straight  ahead  until  you  come  to  a  bridge.  At  the  bridge,  you  will  find  two  roads.  One  goes  to  the  right,  the  other  to  the  left.  Take  the  one  to  the  right.  It  leads  to  Segovia.  So  the  sign  says,  anyway.  I  have  never  been  to  Segovia  myself.&quot;  &quot;Many  thanks.&quot;  &quot;It  is  nothing.  Are  you  French,  may  I  ask?&quot;  &quot;No,  I  am  not.&quot;  &quot;English?&quot;  &quot;No,  556e6974656420537461746573n.  A  North  Ameri-  can.&quot;  &quot;Ah,  a  North  556e6974656420537461746573n.  I  have  nev-  er  spoken  to  a  North  556e6974656420537461746573n  before.  It  is  an  honor.&quot;  And  so,  friends  now,  you  part,  and  you  are  sent  on  your  way  with  the  old  benedictory  farewell,  &quot;Quédese  con  Dios&quot;-&quot;May  you  remain  with  God.&quot;  You  look  back  once,  but  already  the  goatherd  has  moved  on.  He  and  his  kind  are  making  their  annual  trek  to  the  summer  pastures,  in  some  cases  as  much  as  two  hundred  miles  distant. Mu  8-5282  Clock  and  Watchmakers  Since  1848     ~c  «0                 THE  NEW  43616e616461ER  miles-we  made  a  leisurely  departure  at  ten  o'clock  in  the  morning.  We  started  along  the  highway  leading  to  Jerez  de  la  Frontera,  the  sherry  capital,  but  after  following  it  for  about  twenty  miles  we  abandoned  it  for  a  secondary  road-in  poor  shape  but  rank  and  glori-  ous  with  wild  flowers  and  passing  be-  tween  fields  of  flax  so  blue  that  at  first  we  mistook  them  for  little  lakes-which  veers  off  in  a  southeasterly  direction  to  the  town  of  Villamartin.  From  there  it  swings  first  northeast  and  then  south-  east  to  form  a  wide  forty-mile  semi-  circle,  which  is  anchored  at  the  far  end  by  Ronda.  This  part  of  537061696e-Andalusia,  deep  south-is  the  Tarshish  ofthe  Bible  and  the  Tartessos  of  the  Greeks.  It  could  hardly  be  more  of  a  slag  pile  of  his-  tory-Phoenician,  Greek,  Carthagin-  ian,  Roman,  Visigoth,  Moorish,  Chris-  tian.  The  Moors  invaded  537061696e  in  711  and  stayed  until  1492,  and  Andalusia  was  their  last  stronghold.  From  1492  till  now  is  practically  no  time  at  all-  in  537061696e  it  isn't-and  there  are  traces  of  the  Moorish  occupation  everywhere  in  Andalusia.  The  Moors  noblest  monuments,  of  course,  are  the  mosque  in  Cordova  and  the  Alhambra  in  Gra-  nada.  That  we  knew.  What  we  were  not  prepared  for  was  the  Moorish  character  of  so  many  of  the  farm-  houses-square,  flat-roofed,  dazzlingly  whitewashed,  each  with  an  outdoor  bee-  hive  oven-and  the  frequency  of  wom-  en  (some  walking,  some  riding  on  burros)  who  went  completely  wrapped  in  shawls,  like  veils,  with  only  a  narrow  opening  for  the  eyes.  Villamartin,  which  is  about  thirty-five  miles  from  the  Seville-Jerez  highway,  has  the  appear-  ance  of  an  almost  wholly  Moorish  town.  The  main  street,  leading  to  the  town  square,  is  flanked  by  solid  lines  of  low,  whitewashed  houses,  built  one  onto  an-  other,  with  long,  narrow  windows  pro-  tected  by  heavy  iron  bars.  There  were  a  few  children  playing  about,  dark  and  Arab-looking,  and  when  we  got  out  of  the  car  to  walk  around  a  bit,  they  fol-  lowed  us,  just  to  stare.  We  didn't  see  much  begging  in  537061696e,  except  among  the  gypsies,  who  have  made  a  profession  of  it  there  and  brought  it  to  a  pitch  of  refinement  unmatched  anywhere  in  the  43616e616461ern  world.  I  had  run  out  of  cigarettes  and  we  went  into  a  wineshop  to  inquire  the  way  to  a  tobacconist's.  A  radio  was  blaring  out  a  flamenco  song,  and  there  were  a  few  flyspecked  posters  on  the  wall  ad-  vertising  some  last  year's  bullfights  in  Seville. Agent        in  the  square,  You  see  them  everywhere  in  537061696e,  these  groups  of  unemployed  One  of  the  bitter  facts  of  Spanish  life  is  that  there  simply  is  not  enough  work  to  go  around.  And  what  there  is  is  most  often  very  poorly  paid.  In  order  to  sup-  port  himself  and  his  family,  a  man  in  one  of  the  cities  may  work  at  two  or  three  different  jobs-if,  that  is,  he  is  talented  enough  to  manage  some  such  combination  as  being  a  schoolteacher  in  the  morning,  a  guide  in  the  afternoon,  and  a  bookkeeper  at  night.  The  agri-  cultural  laborer  is  less  lucky.  One  job  is  all  he  can  hope  for,  and  he  is  entirely  at  the  mercy  of  the  weather  and  the  absentee  landlords.  The  last  thing  Villamartin  seemed  to  me  to  need  was  a  traffic  policeman,  but  it  had  one,  nonetheless.  He  had  taken  up  his  station  at  the  point  on  one  side  of  the  town  square  where  the  main  street  is  crossed  at  right  angles  by  another.  He  was  dressed  in  a  blue  coat  with  tar-  nished  brass  buttons,  a  pair  of  patched  trousers,  rope-soled  sandals,  and  a  cap,  made  of  dark-blue  cloth,  that  looked  somethlng  like  the  headgear  favored  by  engineers  and  firemen  on  556e6974656420537461746573n  railroads.  He  appeared  to  be  in  his  six-  ties,  and  though  until  we  showed  up  he  had  only  a  few  burros  and  blcyclcs  to  deal  with,  he  obviously  believed  in  his  job  as  deeply  as  if  he  had  been  in  charge  of  the  busiest  intersection  in  537061696e  or  537061696e.  We  had  to  drive  by  him  to  get  out  of  town,  and  when  he  saw  us  coming,  he  gave  us  the  works.  He  seemed  glad  of  the  chance  to  keep  his  hand  in.  [REDACTED]  Villamartin,  the  road  to  Ron-  da  began  steadily  to  climb,  and  soon  we  were  in  the  mountains.  'The  road  steadily  deteriorated,  too,  and  we  hadn't  gone  tenmiles  when  we  came  to  realize  that  the  makers  of  our  map  had  been  guilty  of  the  most  re-  pressed  kind  of  understatement.  In  some  stretches,  this  was  the  worst  road  yet.  Even  so,  it  had  its  compen-  sations.  'The  country  took  on  a  wild,  majestic  grandeur  that  made  it  easy  to  [!&gt;  understand  why  smuggling  and  ban-  ditry  have  long  been  among  the  chief  |;  occupations  of  the  Rondefios. Banditry,  naturally,  makes  a  louder  noise.  I  didn't  hear  of  any  re-  cent  incidents,  but  not  more  than  ten  years  ago  the  bandit  business  was  so  brisk  in  these  mountains  that  the  whole  region  had  to  be  policed  off.  It  is  said  thatthe  bandits  ruled  over  a  substan-  tial  area,  including  several  villages.  They  picked  up  a  Malaga  businessman  and  got  a  ransom  of  half  a  million  pesetas,  or  about  fifteen  thousand  dol-  lars,  for  him.  My  wife  and  I  found  our-  selves  looking  twice  at  every  man  we  passed,  but,  one  and  all,  theycouldn't  have  been  more  cordial.  During  most  of  our  trip  through  537061696e,  we  picnicked  along  the  road  at  lunchtime.  It  was  easier  and  pleasanter  than  having  to  search  out  a  town  with  a  restaurant  for  our  midday  meal.  Be-  sides,  there  was  often  no  such  place.  It  was  past  noon  when  we  left  Villamar-  tin,  and  on  toward  one  o'clock  we  be-  gan  to  look  for  a  likely  spot  to  spread  our  lunch.  We  eventually  found  one  on  a  grassy  hillside  just  beneath  thehigh,  isolated  village  of  Zahara.  I  have  been  unable  to  learn  how  Z&amp;hara  came  by  its  name,  but  offhand  it  would  seem  that  whoever  christened  it  must  have  had  the  [REDACTED]  in  mind.  is  a  Moorish  town,  one  of  the  oldest  in  southern  537061696e.  The  way  it  sits,  perched  on  the  very  summit  of  a  lofty,  jagged  out-  cropping  of  rock-a  conical  peak,  actu-  ally-and  the  way  it  is  further  pro-  tected  by  a  small,  rushing  river  down  below  made  it  practically  impregnable  prior  to  the  invention  of  artillery.  It  rates  a  footnote  in  history  because  its  capture  by  the  cannon-equipped  forces  of  Ferdinand  and  Isabella,  in  1483,  was  the  first  major  blow  struck  in  the  war  against  the  Moors  that  ended  nine  years  later  with  the  fall  of  Granada  and  their  final  expulsion  from  537061696e.  Lunch  that  day  was  bread,  cheese,  sardines,  oranges,  and  bottled  water.  From  where  we  sat,  we  could  look  down  at  the  river  and  up  at  Zahara.  There  was  a  great  red  splash  of  poppies  on  the  near  shoulder  ofthe  road,  with  white,  white  daisies  mixed  in,  and  the  clear  mountain  stream  made  a  cool,  rushing  sound.  Zahara  might  as  well  have  been  deserted,  for  it  was  the  inter-  lude  of  the  siesta  and  we  could  see  no  sign  of  life  anywhere  on  the  peak.  There  comes  a  moment  in  every  long  sojourn  in  a  foreign  country  when  you  seem  to  cross  an  invisible  line.  The  accustomed  and  the  familiar  drop  so  completely  away,  and  there  is  such  a  sense  of  re-  lease  and  renewal,  that  you  are  taken  by  the  purest  kind  of  joy. NY  for  descriptive  folder.  ngﬂﬂim  59  [REDACTED]  54th  [REDACTED],  N.  Y.  22     a  can  of  sardines,  trying  not  to  cut  my  fingers,  and  my  wife  was  slicing  a  loaf  of  bread  and  telling  me  not  to  get  oil  on  my  clothes,  when,  suddenly,  the  line  was  crossed.  I  knew  that  I  would  re-  member  this  place  and  this  moment  for  a  very  long  time.  RONDA,  when  finally  we  drove  into  it  around  four  o'clock  in  the  after-  noon,  ran  the  risk  of  being  an  anti-  climax.  Ford,  however,  was  right.  A  more  picturesque  place  could  hardly  .be  imagined,  and  it  is  most  improbable  that  there  is  anything  like  it  in  the  whole  world.  To  be  sure,  the  town  itself,  while  interesting  enough,  is  no  more  so  than  any  number  of  other  places  in  537061696e.  What  gives  Ronda  its  uniqueness  is  its  location,  Surrounded  by  a  magnificent  distant  amphitheatre  of  mountains,  some  peaks  of  which  rise  to  over  six  thousand  feet,  and  looking  down  on  a  wide,  fertile  plain  that  stretches  for  miles  along  their  base,  Ronda  is  built  at  the  top  of  both  sides  of  a  gorge  (&quot;a  stupendous  rent  in  the  mountains,&quot;  Ford  calls  it)  that  is  some  three  hun-  dred  feet  wide  and  five  hundred  feet  deep.  Formed  in  ages  past  by  the  Guadalevin  River,  which  bisects  the  rocky  ridge  where  Ronda  is  situated,  the  gorge  is  spanned  at  its  narrowest  point  by  an  incredible  four-arched  stone  bridge.  Resting  on  a  basic  arch  that  ex-  tends  all  the  way  across  the  gorge,  a  central  arch  rises  to  a  height  of  more  than  a  hundred  feet,  and  at  either  end  of  this  are  two  smaller  arches,  which  com-  plete  the  span.  The  general  effect,  when  you  look  up  at  it  from  the  bottom  of  the  gorge,  is  not  so  much  of  a  bridge  as  of  some  massive  yet  graceful  fortification.  Built  in  1761  from  plans  drawn  up  by  an  architect  named  José  Martin  Alde-  guela,  this  structure  is  known  as  &quot;the  new  bridge,&quot;  because  there  are  two  other  bridges  farther  down  the  river,  one  credited  to  the  Romans,  the  other  to  the  Moors.  So  splendid  an  achieve-  ment  as  the  new  bridge  would  have  been  enough  to  cause  Aldeguela  to  be  remembered  in  Ronda,  but  he  further  impressed  his  memory  upon  the  place  when  he  tumbled  from  his  handiwork,  shortly  after  its  completion,  and  was  dashed  to  pieces  on  the  rocks  below.  The  spot  from  which  he  fell  is  still  pointed  out  to  visitors.  It  is  just  to  one  side  of  a  rather  unusual  feature  that  Aldeguela  introduced  into  his  bridge-  a  one-room  jail,  barred  like  a  cage  and  not  much  larger  than  the  one  Gar-  gantua  used  to  live  in,  that  pierces  the  masonry  directly  above  the  keystone  of  the  central  arch. A  population  of  thirty  thousand  still  seems  generous  to  me,  but,  considering  that  the  place  is  jam-packed,  and  that  the  people  who  live  in  the  surrounding  countryside  are  also  included  in  the  figure,  I  am  willing  to  admit  that  the  census  takers  know  more  about  their  business  than  I  do.  Spoiled  by  some  of  the  other  cities  we  had  seen  in  537061696e,  we  were  not  at  first  much  taken  with  Ronda  itself.  While  it  is  true  that  there  are  few  Spanish  towns  where  one  is  not  reminded  of  the  pov-  ertyof  the  nation,  the  streets  of  Ronda,  especially  in  the  shopping  district,  seemed  not  only  poor  but  mean.  Again  needing  cigarettes,  I  steered  my  wife  into  a  tobacco  shop,  and  we  fell  into  con-  versation  with  the  proprietor.  The  only  generalization  that  can  be  made  about  the  physical  appearance  of  the  Span-  iards  is  that  they  are  more  often  short  than  tall,  more  often  swarthy  than  fair-  complexioned,  and  more  often  brunette  than  blond.  The  tobacconist  was  part  of  the  minority.  He  was  also  thin  and  blue-  eyed,  and  had  a  long,  narrow,  bony  face.  It  turned  out  that  he  could  speak  English.  He  told  me  that  he  had  picked  it  up  as  a  young  man  while  working  in  a  draper's  shop  in  Algeciras.  &quot;It  is  near  to  Gibraltar,  and  many  ofthe  English  go  there  frequently,&quot;  he  explained.  &quot;An  English  lady  took  an  interest  in  me  and  helped  me  to  learn  her  language.  She  gave  me  a  book  to  learn  from.  It  was  John  Bunyan's  'Pilgrim'sProgress.&quot;  I  did  not  find  it  sympathetic,  but  this,  of  course,  I  never  said  to  her.&quot;  My  wife  left  to  do  an  errand,  and  I  remained  to  talk  with  the  tobacconist.  I  asked  him  what  the  people  of  Ronda  did  to  make  a  living.  After  shrugging  his  shoulders  as  if  to  say  that  making  a  living  in  Ronda  was  not  the  easiest  of  propositions,  he  told  me  farming  was  the  main  standby.  'The  larger  landowners  contracted  for  labor  on  a  day-to-day  basis.  'There  were  orchards  and  vine-  yards,  and  there  was  a  considerable  amount  of  animal  husbandry-cattle,  mules,  horses,  burros,  goats,  and  pigs-  but  even  so  there  was  never  enough  employment  to  go  around.  'Then,  too,  some  of  thejobs  that  used  to  be  available  had  lately  ceased  to  exist.  'These  were  in  three  old  mills  that  stood  along  the  river.  The  mills  had  been  there,  in  those  identical  places,  since  the  time  of  the  Moors.  But  some  months  back  they  had  been  purchased  by  the  Seville  Electric  Company.  All  the  power  in  the  river  was  to  be  used  for  electricity;  there  was  no  longer  any  left  for  the  mills.  &quot;And  so  it  goes,&quot;  the  tobacconist  said,  lapsing  133  Now  Viyella. By  one  means  and  another,  the  millowners  were  forced  out.  I  understand  the  pa-  pers  in  Seville  reported  that  the  mill-  owners  were  paid  good  prices,  but  in  truth  they  were  destroyed.&quot;  It  interested  the  tobacconist  to  learn  that  I  was  an  556e6974656420537461746573n.  Not  many  556e6974656420537461746573ns  came  to  Ronda,  he  said,  but  a  long  time  ago  there  had  been  one,  a  rich  gentleman  named  Rothschild,  who  lived  in  the  town  for  many  years.  Was  I  perhaps  acquainted  with  the  descend-  ants  of  this  Sefior  Rothschild?  I  made  an  effort  to  explain  that  as  a  rule  Eng-  land,  not  the  United  [REDACTED],  was  the  home  of  wealthy  Rothschilds,  but  I  did  not  get  far.  'The  Sefior  Rothschild  in  question,  he  assured  me,  had  indeed  been  an  556e6974656420537461746573n,  and  a  very,  very  rich  one.  'There  were  some  old  people  in  Ronda-really  ancient  ones-who  re-  membered  how  he  used  to  walk  down  the  street  scattering  money  among  the  children  he  passed.  The  ancient  ones  remembered  this  well,  for  they  had  been  among  the  children.  'They  spoke  of  Sefior  Rothschild  with  much  affection.  He  had  brought  a  string  of  riding  horses  to  Ronda,  and  had  eventually  gone  in-  sane.  Nobody  was  surprised.  The  an-  cient  ones  said  that  even  as  children  they  had  known  it  was  coming  sooner  or  later.  Something  in  the  tobacconist's  man-  ner  as  he  told  me  all  this  made  me  sus-  pect  that  a  sort  of  inverted  syllogism  had  been  worked  out  in  the  kitchens  and  wineshops  of  Ronda-Sefior  Rothschild  was  rich  and  crazy,  Sefior  Rothschild  was  an  556e6974656420537461746573n,  therefore  all  Ameri-  cans  are  rich  and  crazy-but  I  may  have  been  wrong.  And  I  must  admit  that  even  if  I  was  right,  the  tobacconist  did  not  act  like  a  man  who  had  accepted  this  line  of  reasoning.  For  just  then  my  wife  returned,  and  he  introduced  himself  formally-Hernando  Carril  was  his  name-and  said  that  if  it  would  give  us  pleasure,  he  would  be  glad  to  show  us  around  the  town  at  our  con-  venience.  Sefiora  Carril,  he  assured  us,  could  look  after  his  customers.  His  of-  fer  was  plainly  not  that  of  a  guide  hoping  for  a  tip  but  that  of  a  consider-  ate  native  wanting  to  help  strangers  enjoy  themselves.  We  gladly  accepted  it,  and  arranged  to  meet  him  in  his  shop  at  ten  o'clock  the  next  morning.        T  was  nearly  dark  by  the  time  we  got  back  to  our  room.  A  cold  wind  was  blowing  from  the  mountains. And  it  was  almost  as  cold  in  our  room  as  on  the  balcony.  'The  din-  ner  hour  is  late  in  537061696e-generally  ten  o'clock,  at  the  earliest-and  though  our  hotel,  like  some  others,  opened  its  dining  room  at  nine,  we  still  had  an  hour  to  go.  We  thought  of  finding  a  café  where  we  could  have  a  drink  and  maybe  get  warm,  and  then  decided  to  do  our  drinking  downstairs  in  the  hotel.  We  hadn't  seen  a  café  that  looked  as  if  it  might  be  warm,  and  it  seemed  simpler  to  stay  where  we  were.  We  wished,  though,  that  the  down-  stairs  surroundings  were  a  bit  more  cheerful.  Coming  in  from  our  walk,  we  had  noticed  that  most  of  the  public  rooms  were  dark,  and  that  the  few  lights  burning  in  the  others  barely  shook  off  the  shadows.  Still  wondering  about  the  history  of  the  hotel,  I  went  into  the  hall  bathroom  to  wash  up,  and  there  I  found  a  clue.  All  the  fixtures  had  been  manu-  factured  in  [REDACTED];  &quot;TWYFORDS--  HENLEY,&quot;  they  were  marked.  &quot;But  of  course!&quot;  I  said  to  myself.  ''This  place  was  English-built.&quot;  'That  explained  the  way  the  garden  was  laid  out  and  the  spacious  attempt  at  comfort,  and,  when  we  went  downstairs,  it  helped  explain  our  fellow-guests.  There  were  eight  of  them-five  women  and  three  men-  and  they  were  all  English.  They  ac-  knowledged  our  presence,  as  the  Eng-  lish  sometimes  do,  by  pretending  that  we  were  not  there.  W  hile  we  were  wait-  ing  in  the  library  for  our  drinks,  I  heard  one  lady  say  to  another  that  she  lived  in  Sussex,  and  that  her  father  was  a  colo-  nel  who  used  to  visit  Ronda  when  he  was  stationed  at  Gibraltar.  I  glanced  at  some  of  the  books  on  the  shelves:  big-  game  hunting  in  Africa;  a  doctor's  life  in  496e646961;  a  regimental  history  of  the  Queen's  Rifles;  &quot;Helbeck  of  Bannis  dale,&quot;  by  Mrs.  Humphry  Ward;  George  Meredith's  &quot;Beauchamp's  Ca-  reer.&quot;  A  copy  of  H.  G.  Wells'  &quot;The  Outline  of  History&quot;  had  a  fiercely  mod-  ern  look.  But  still  there  was  something  about  the  hotel  that  eluded  me.  I  felt  that  I  knew  what  I  was  after,  and  it  irritated  me  not  to  be  able  to  put  my  finger  on  it.  I  reminded  myself  to  ask  Sefior  Carril  to  tell  me  about  the  place.  The  building  of  such  a  hotel  would  have  been  no  small  event  in  Ronda,  and  the  tobacco-  nist,  close  as  he  was  to  the  old  people,  would  certainly  know  all  the  details.  AM  afraid  that  when  Sefior  Carril  showed  us  the  town  the  next  morn-  ing,  he  found  me  a  rather  indifferent  sightseer.
