Johnson  &amp;  Sons,  43616e616461Haven,  [REDACTED]  PACIHC  MILLS  261  FIFTH  AVENUE,  NEW  [REDACTED]  16,  N.  Y.  A  member  of  Burlington  Industries,  Inc.  Burlington  cate  wrought-iron  balcony  in  the  high-  rococo  style.  The  ring,  built  in  two  tiers,  differs  from  all  others  in  that  it  has  a  canopy,  made  of  wood,  over  its  seats.  The  circular  procession  of  precisely  spaced  slender  columns  supporting  the  arches  that,  in  turn,  support  the  cano-  py  gives  Ronda's  ring  an  intimacy  and  coziness  that  must  make  a  bullfight  there  seem  almost  a  family  entertain-  ment.  I  have  to  say  must  because  there  were  no  fights  while  we  were  in  Ronda.  The  Ronda  ring  seats  ten  thousand  spectators.  Once  the  largest  ring  in  the  country,  it  is  now  one  ofthe  smallest.  Indeed,  the  arena  proper  is  so  small  that  Rondefios  scornfully  accuse  the  big-  city  bullfighters  of  being  afraid  to  appear  there  because,  with  so  little  room  for  maneuvering,  they  run  too  great  a  risk  of  being  gored.  I  could  see  that  my  friend  Sefior  Carril  felt  strongly  about  the  matter,  and  that  only  my  wife's  presencekept  him  from  letting  go  com-  pletely.  But  it  could  be  that  the  issue  is  more  one  of  cash  than  of  courage.  On  the  basis  ofthe  fees  that  a  ranking  bull-  fighter  can  command  in  bigger  arenas,  Ronda  must  be  placed  in  the  category  ofa  tank  town.  The  bull  ring  is  con-  sequently  used  but  once  a  year,  on  May  20th,  when  everybody  turns  out  for  the  start  of  a  three-day  ferie  com-  memorating  the  Moors'  surrender  of  Ronda  to  the  Christians.  Y  interest  in  the  bull  ring  had  not  caused  me  to  lose  interest  in  the  hotel.  When,  finally,  our  tour  was  over  and  we  were  accompanying  Sefior  Car-  ril  back  to  his  shop,  I  asked  him  about  it.  He  seemed  surprised  by  my  curiosity.  Yes,  he  knew  the  history  of  the  hotel.  It  was  built  in  1898  by  an  Englishman  named  McInton-a  retired  mining  en-  gineer  who  had  settled  in  Gibraltar.  There  were  many  in  Ronda  who  re-  membered  him  well.  He  was  a  tall  man,  with  reddish  hair  and  blue  eyes,  who  never  really  learned  to  speak  Spanish.  In  this  respect,  he  was  not  found  as  sympathetic  as  Sefior  Rothschild.  Sefior  Rothschild  spokeSpanish  with  grace  and  authority.  'The  old  people  con-  sidered  him  just  as  well-bred  as  they  were.  &quot;But  the  hotel,&quot;  I  said.  &quot;How  did  McInton  come  to  build  it?&quot;  Sefior  Carril  shrugged.  &quot;When  he  first  came  to  Ronda,  staying  at  an  inn  near  the  bull  ring,  nobody  thought  of  him  as  a  rich  man,&quot;  he  said.  &quot;How-  ever,  when  he  started  building  the  ho-  tel,  pouring  into  it  only  God  knows  how  many  millions  of  pesetas,  it  began  to  be  rumored  that  he  was  nearly  as  rich  as  Sefior  Rothschild. Who  knows?&quot;  It  was  easy  to  see  that  McInton  was  not  remembered  quite  as  affectionately  in  Ronda  as  Sefior  Rothschild.  I  asked  a  few  more  questions,  but  Sefior  Carril  had  run  out  of  information.  All  he  could  tell  me  was  that  the  hotel  was  believed  to  be  having  trouble  making  ends  meet,  'There  had  been  a  time,  he  said,  when  many  English  people  came  to  Ronda-especially  the  English  who  lived  on  Gibraltar-but  lately  fewer  had  been  coming  every  year.  It  had  something  to  do  with  their  money,  he  added,  though  what  this  was  he  didn't  know.  He  gave  it  as  his  opinion  that  if  things  kept  on  the  way  they  were  going,  the  hotel  would  have  to  close  its  doors.  This  I  had  already  suspected.  But  what  I  was  searching  for  was  some-  thing  else-something  that  I  still  could  not  quite  define.  We  left  Sefior  Carril  at  his  shop,  went  to  the  hotel  for  lunch,  and  then  took  a  walk  in  the  country.  Arriving  back  at  the  hotel  around  four  o'clock,  we  found  that  our  walk  had  made  us  hungry,  so  we  ordered  a  small  meal  of  bread,  butter,  jam,  and  coffee,  and  ate  it  on  the  hotel's  piazza,  looking  out  across  the  garden  at  the  view.  There  are  hundreds  of  swallows  in  Ronda,  making  their  homes  in  the  cracks  and  crevices  of  the  gorge,  and  some  of  them  were  flying  about.  A  big  green  lizard-the  biggest  lizard  I  have  ever  seen-was  sunning  itself  on  one  of  the  garden  paths.  For  a  while,  there  wasn't  a  sound.  We  watched  the  swallows  and  the  liz-  ard,  and  I  don't  think  that  I  could  have  felt  myself  to  be  deeper  in  537061696e.  Then,  from  inside  the  hotel,  someone  searched  out  a  few  chords  on  a  piano.  They  seemed  actually  to  break  through  the  silence,  leaving  a  ripped,  shattered  place.  After  a  pause,  the  piano  was  struck  more  purposefully  and  a  soprano  voice  began  singing  &quot;On  the  Road  to  Man-  dalay.&quot;  The  voice  and  the  song  hung  on  the  edge  of  absurdity  for  a  mo-  ment-Oh,  no!  Not  here!  Not  in  this  eagle's  nest  of  a  town  high  in  the  moun-  tains  of  537061696e'-but  then,  when  the  singer,  flatting  just  a  little,  got  to  the  tricky  part  where  the  dawn  comes  up  like  thunder  out  of  4368696e61  across  the  bay,  the  voice  and  the  song,  instead  of  seem-  ing  absurd,  were  exactly  in  place  and  entirely  correct.  The  elusive  thing  that  I  had  been  searching  for  finally  came  to  me,  and  I  wondered  how  I  could  have  been  so  long  in  grasping  it. THE  NEW  43616e616461ER  daughter  of  an  English  colonel  who  had  once  been  stationed  at  Gibraltar),  was  one  of  the  last  reminders  of  the  great  days  of  European  travel-the  period,  extending  up  to  1900,  or  even  a  decade  later-when  if  one  ventured  into  537061696e  it  was  still  possible  to  be  rewarded  with  something  of  the  same  sense  of  per-  sonal  discovery  that  such  earlier  travel-  lers  as  Borrow  and  Ford  experienced.  The  world  hadn't  yet  shrunk.  It  took  a  certain  amount  of  doing  to  get  from  [REDACTED]  to  the  peninsula,  and  an  even  greater  amount  of  doing  to  get  to  Ron-  da.  After  such  a  trial,  one  wanted  to  settle  down  for  a  while.  One  was  grate-  ful  to  Mr.  McInton  for  making  things  almost  as  nice  as  they  were  at  home.  There  were  picnics,  walks  in  the  hills,  diaries  to  be  written  up,  new  novels  like  &quot;Helbeck  of  Bannisdale&quot;  to  be  read,  the  pleasure  of  the  post  when  it  ar-  rived,  and  simply  the  thrill  of  knowing  (and  here  I  see  a  covey  of  dainty,  chat-  tering  girls  in  fresh  white  muslin  dresses  cut  on  princesse  lines,  and  wide  straw  hats  with  colored  ribbons,  pink  and  yel-  lowand  blue)  that  there  were  bandits  and  brigands  in  the  hills-dark,  lean,  romantically  handsome  men  with  white  teeth,  who  played  guitars.  It  was  like  that  then,  and  there  is  no  reason  to  believe  that  it  will  ever  be  like  that  again.  People  just  won't  take  the  time  or  make  the  effort.  Travel  has  become  tourism,  and  is  ruled  by  the  clock.  There  are  buses  that  cover  the  whole  Grand  Tour  in  a  little  over  a  month.  It's  the  tourist  trade  that  Eu-  rope  is  now  geared  for,  and  even  537061696e  is  beginning  to  succumb.  In  Torre-  molinos,  a  seaside  resort  near  Mélaga,  I  saw  a  roadside  restaurant  that  was  built  to  resemble  a  battleship;  I  thought  it  would  look  rather  extreme  in  Holly-  wood,  but  since  I  was  only  getting  back  what  we  export,  I  couldn't  very  well  quarrel  with  it.  I  am  afraid,  however,  that  it  is  part  of  a  trend.  Any  number  of  new  hotels  have  been  built  in  537061696e  recently,  a  few  so  slick  and  shiny  that  they  might  have  sprung  full-chromed  from  the  brain  of  Mr.  [REDACTED]  [REDACTED]  himself,  and  many  others  are  going  up.  The  Costa  Brava-the  part  of  537061696e's  Mediterranean  coastline  that  runs  north  from  537061696e  to  the  French  border-  is  having  a  small-sized  [REDACTED]  boom.  So  it  does  not  seem  unlikelythat  my  friend  Sefior  Carril,  and  some  other  hardheaded  citizens  of  Ronda  that  I  spoke  with,  are  right.  Ronda-miles  away  from  the  tourist  run-still  isn't  the  easiest  place  in  the  world  to  get  to  and  besides,  who  would  want  to  go  to  all  that  trouble  just  to  look  at  a  view&quot;  I  do  not  think  that  these  people  of  Ron-  141  p  a  p  e  r  p  l  a  t  e  c  o  o  k  i  n  g  in  TAPPANM  cold-oven  electronic  range! THE  RACE  TRACK  Moving  Day  ‘  ‘  Z  TTH  Saratoga  over,  racing  moves  into  its  au-  tumnal  stage-the  most  interesting  of  *  the  year,  and  often  the  pleasantest.I  venture  to  say,  how-  ever,  that  this  time  around  lots  of  horse-  players  have  been  feeling  that  something  is  wrong-that  they  really  ought  to  be  at  Aqueduct  this  week,  instead  of  at  [REDACTED]  [REDACTED].  And  they're  such  slaves  to  habit  that  it's  8-5some  of  them  have  gone  halfway  to  Aqueduct  at  least  once  before  they  remembered.  Since  the  [REDACTED]  meeting  will  continue  through  October  1  5th,  the  management  has  bor-  rowed  several  of  Aqueduct's  stake  races  and  scheduled  the  more  important  ones  for  Saturdays;  the  Beldame,  for  instance,  will  be  on  September  22nd  and  the  Cow  din  on  September  29th.  This  week-  end,  one  of  [REDACTED]'s  own  older  fix-  tures-the  Jerome  Handicap,  for  three-  year-olds-will  have  its  eighty-seventh  running.  The  starters  will  be  no  great  shakes.  «  La  F  all  the  races  that  have  disap-  peared  from  the  Spa,  the  one  I  miss  most  is  the  Saratoga  Cup,  which  was  discontinued  this  year.  Though  it  was  never  as  enthralling  as  the  Sub-  urban-usually  it  brought  outonly  four  or  five  runners-it  seemed  to  me  a  sym-  bol  ofthe  old,  spacious  days.  Oh,  well,  times  change.  Even  the  exodus  is  differ-  ent.  There  used  to  be  a  special  train  to  take  the  horses  back  to  Long  Island,  but  now  most  of  them  go  in  motor  vans-  huge  contraptions  that  carry  as  many  racers  as  an  express  car  and  make  the  trip  much  quicker.  It  always  fascinates  me  to  watch  a  big  stable  get  ready  to  move,  particularly  at  Saratoga.  The  beds  and  blankets,  the  bales  and  bags  of  fodder,  the  medicine  chest  and  the  band-  age  box,  the  buckets,  brooms,  tubs,  and  rakes-all  painted  in  the  stable's  racing  colors  (not  so  much  for  ostentatious  dis-  play  as  to  keep  grooms  for  other  stables  from  borrowing  them)-are  packed  wk.~  with  circus-train  precision,  the  blanketed  3  and  bandaged  racers  are  loaded,  the  }=  men  climb  in,  and  away  they  go,  roaring  22  down  the  Thruway. N  &quot;Gestapo&quot;  (Viking),  Mr.  Edward  Crankshaw  has  written  what  is,  so  long  as  it  sticks  to  fact,  by  far  the  best  history  that  has  yet  appeared  of  the  Geheime  Staatspolizei,  or  Secret  State  Police,  set  up  by  Goring  in  1933  and  taken  overby  Himmler  a  year  later.  As  Mr.  Crankshaw  points  out,  this  organization  was,  before  Gor-  ing  assumed  command  and  renamed  it,  Department  IA  of  the  old  Prussian  Political  Police,  an  instrument  of  gov-  ernment  that  was  originally  the  equiva-  lent  of  the  F.B.I.  and  the  Special  Branch  of  the  British  C.I.D.  Depart-  ment  IA,  like  these  556e6974656420537461746573n  and  British  institutions,  had  made  itself  an  underground  arm  of  government,  so  that  it  could  combat  underground  sub-  versive  movements  on  equal  terms.  It  kept  its  methods,  its  personnel,  its  rec-  ords,  and  its  expenditures  secret  for  reasons  that  appeared  well  founded,  and  under  that  cloak  became  in  timea  law  unto  itself,  answerable  neither  to  the  Reichstag  nor  to  the  German  peo-  ple  butonly  to  whoever  held  the  office  of  Prussian  Minister  of  the  Interior.  W  hen  Goring  took  it  over,  along  with  its  vo-  luminous  files,  secretly  compiled  from  secret  sources,  on  Germans  active  or  in-  fluential  in  politics,  it  was  an  apparatus  made  to  order  for  his  special  purpose  of  singling  out  and  breaking  those  who  were  still  opposed  to  Hitler  and  Hitler-  ism.  As  used  by  Goring,  Department  IA  rapidly  ceased  to  bear  much  re-  semblance  to  the  F.B.I.  or  the  Special  Branch,  and  the  simi-  larity  disappeared  alto-  gether  when  Himmler  supplanted  Goring's  simplehandicraft  ma-  chine  with  a  great  in-  dustrial  organization  devoted  to  the  terrori-  zation  and  extermina-     tion  o  f  whol  e  races  and  cays  social  categories  inside  &gt;  «b  T4  and  outside  [REDACTED].  Mr.  Crankshaw  tells  ~  the  ugly  story  of  the  growth  and  develop-  ment  of  the  hideous  ap-  paratus  brilliantly  and  concisely,  and  lists  its  loathsome  accomplish-  ments  with  judicial  de-  tachment.  But  to  this     a  BOOKS  The  Silver  Men  extremely  valuable  historical  study  he  attaches  a  moral  argument  that  seems  more  than  dubious:  In  all  modern  societies  there  are  men  and  women  who,  released  from  all  re-  straints,  will  behave  like  beasts  towards  their  fellows.  But  in  the  twentieth  century  it  has  been  only  in  [REDACTED]  and  in  [REDACTED]  that  such  men  have  been  able  to  achieve  absolute  power;  and  it  is  only  in  [REDACTED]  that  they  have  deliberately  delegated  their  power  without  reserve  to  psychopaths  and  the  criminal  riffraff  of  their  country,  ab-  solving  them  from  all  restraint.  The  German  system  of  education,  with  its  exaltation  of  unquestioning  obedience  to  authority  and  the  consequent  encour-  agement  of  bullying  on  the  one  hand  and  irresponsibility  on  the  other,  must  contrib-  ute  to  the  sort  of  behaviour  contemplated  in  these  pages.  It  has  taken  the  Germans  themselves  to  develop  this  system.  Have  they  abolished  it?  Mr.  Crankshaw  expands  this  line  of  argument  and  then  proceeds  to  the  in-  dictment  of  a  nation:  Other  people  besidethe  Germans  have  accepted  the  tyranny  ofa  wicked  man;  others  instinctively  regard  certain  cate-  gories  of  their  fellow  men  as  being  in-  ferior  to  the  point  of  being  sub-human.  We  need  go  no  farther  than  Soviet  [REDACTED]  or  Fascist  4974616c79  for  examples  of  the  one;  than  the  British  settlers  in  4b656e7961  or  the  Dutch  of  [REDACTED]  Africa  for  examples  of  the  other.  But  in  no  other  country  has  murder  and  outrage  been  carried  so  far  as  in  Nazi  [REDACTED],  and  in  no  other  country  have  so  many  individuals  from  all  classes  of  society  been  actively  involved  in  murder  and  out-  rage.  This  indictment  is  followed  by  a  ver-  dict  of  guilty  as  charged,  and  the  sen-  tence  he  pronounces  suggests  that  [REDACTED]  should  be  given,  indefinitely,  $  «88  es  145  a  probationary  and  inferior  status  in  the  comity  of  national  states:  But  in  the  last  resort  the  German  fail-  ure,  which  so  far  differentiates  [REDACTED]  from  all  the  other  nations  of  the  [REDACTED],  in-  cluding  [REDACTED],  is  &quot;a  rejection  of  that  re-  ality  which  includes  one's  neighbors,&quot;  and  an  attempt  to  substitute  a  false  abstrac-  tion.  It  is  idealism  gone  rotten;  and  until  they  can  learn  to  accept  a  reality  which  in-  cludes  people,  the  Germans,  in  their  rest-  less  and  insane  striving  for  something  bet-  ter,  will  remain  dangerous  to  those  who  content  themselves  with  trying  to  make  the  best  of  the  world  as  we  know  it  to  be.  This  is  an  attractive  line  for  all  non-  Germans,  because  itis  comfortable.  It  dismisses  the  problem  presented  by  a  monstrous  thing  like  the  Gestapo  as  one  rooted  in  specifically  German  patterns  of  causation,  and  ignores  the  disagree-  able  possibility  that  the  Gestapo  and  its  crimes  are  symptoms  of  a  social  disease  to  which  all  national  states  are  suscepti-  ble  and  which  can  be  held  in  check  only  by  constant  vigilance. Her  Charm  and  grace  are  made  Of  many  things  B  E  R  M  U  D  A  Bermuda  is  only  twenty-four  miles  long  yet  within  its  small  area  are  any  number  of  things  which  charm  visitors  into  go-  ing  back  year  after  year.  Bermuda  is  a  group  of  365  islands.  One  for  each  day  in  the  year,  so  Bermu-  dians  say.  Sailing  among  them  is  a  never-ending  pleasure.  Golf,  tennis,  fishing,  cycling,  picnicking  are  all-year  sports.  Bermuda's  beaches  are  wide,  pink  and  soft.  The  water  is  blue,  invit-  ing  and  refreshing.  Bermuda's  houses  are  unique-an  artful  blending  of  white  roofs,  pastel-coloured  walls  and  mas-  sive  chimneys.  And  flowers-Hibiscus,  Oleander,  Bougainvillea,  43616e616461er  lilies  splash  the  [REDACTED]  with  brilliant  hues.  Bermuda  is  only  700  miles  from  the  mainland.  It's  easy  to  get  to  for  a  holi-  day  whether  you  go  leisurely  by  ocean  liner  or  speedily  by  plane.  The  [REDACTED]'  hotels  are  noted  for  their  service,  food,  accommodations  and  sports  facilities.  And  guest  houses  openwide  their  doors  to  Bermuda's  easy  way  of  living.  There  are  many  details  to  be  con-  sidered  in  planning  any  holiday.  But  you'll  save  yourself  a  lot  of  time  and  trouble  by  talking  things  over  with  your  travel  agent.  Helpful  too  is  the  Bermuda  Vacation  Kit  which  you  can  get  by  writ-  ing  to:  The  Bermuda  Trade  Develop-  ment  Board,  620  Fifth  [REDACTED],  [REDACTED]  20,  N. Known  by  the  Company  it  Keeps  &amp;  (Seagram's  VCO  SEAGRAM  -  DI43616e616461ILIERS  COMPANY,  NEW  [REDACTED]  CITY.  86.8  PROOF,  CANADIAN  WHISKY-  A  BLEND...OF  RARE  SELECTED  WHISKIES  +  SIX  YEARS  OLD  ple  form  of  accountancy  had  a  weak  spot.  Several  tribesmen  turned  up  with-  out  hands  but  quite  alive.  It  was  clear  that  ammunition  was  still  being  wasted,  and  the  system  was  tight-  ened  up;  either  heads  or  male  genital  organs  were  required  as  evidence  that  terrorization  was  proceeding  along  economical  lines.  Under  this  regime,  the  population  of  the  [REDACTED]  declined  in  fifteen  years  from  an  estimated  twenty  million  to  twelve  million.  Ordinary  Belgians,  though  they  could  be  hood-  winked  about  what  was  going  on,  could  hardly  be  expected  to  carry  out  these  ex-  terminations,  and  so  Leopold's  agents  recruited  throughout  Europe  the  dregs  of  its  officer  corps-cashiered  men,  men  with  money  troubles,&quot;  and  men  whose  neuroses  had  brought  their  careers  into  a  blind,  promotionless  alley.  The  parallel  between  this  international  killer  force,  which  was  put  in  command  of  the  native  troops,  and  what  Himmler  made  of  the  S.S.  and  the  Gestapo  in  its  later  stages,  when  it  was  recruiting  personnel  over  all  Europe,  is  an  exact  one,  and  the  ethos  is  also  identical:  a  kind  of  human  being  was  licensed  by  its  supposed  su-  periority  to  do  anything  to  its  supposed  inferiors,  and  relieved  from  moral  re-  sponsibility  because  the  victims  were  in-  feriors,  just  as  slaughterhouse  men  are  exempt  from  criticism  because  what  they  do  is  done  to  &quot;mere  animals.&quot;  The  important  thing  about  the  Bel-  gian  manifestation  of  the  Gestapo  spirit  is  that  it  occurred  under  tranquil  condi-  tions,  when  no  acute  social  pressures  were  involved.  This  suggests  that  the  disease  is  endemic,  and  that  it  is  not  created  by  war  and  defeat  but  is  apt  to  show  up  when  the  society  involved  is  fatigued.  The  United  [REDACTED]  and  the  major  European  powers  suffered  more  or  less  severe  attacks  of  the  complaint  after  the  First  World  War.  The  philo-  sophy  of  the  Ku  Klux  Klan,  its  para-  military  organization,  its  racism,  and  its  deeds  show  that  our  society,  like  any  other,  always  has  the  seeds  of  a  Gestapo  in  it.  The  English  Black  and  Tans  and  the  sinister  Cadets  associated  with  them  in  the  work  of  breaking  the  Irish  be-  tween  1920  and  1922  provide  another  parallel;  Sir  Hamar  Greenwood,  the  ex-temperance  lecturer  and  carnival-  sideshow  barker  who  was  in  charge  of  these  outfits,  was  indistinguishable  from  Himmler,  and  if  he  kept  his  philosophy  to  himself,  his  actions  speak  for  its  na-  ture.  The  spirit  of  the  Black  and  Tans  thrived  in  the  moral  exhaustion  brought  on  by  the  long  strain  of  the  war. This  involved  compiling  a  list  of  influential  and  beloved  Irishmen  and  killing  them  off,  from  the  top  down,  until  the  Irish  willto  resist  &quot;pacification&quot;  was  dead.  If  this  could  happen  in  a  victorious  country  with  a  long  tradition  of  demo-  cratic  control  of  the  executive,  it  is  not  surprising  that  in  a  country  like  Ger-  many,  defeated,  exhausted,  racked  by  attempts  at  revolution,  and  faced  with  insoluble  economic  problems,  the  Ger-  man  Free  Corps  should  have  taken  deeper  root,  consumed  the  legal  police,  and  grown  into  the  Gestapo.  Chauvin-  ists  may  like  to  think  that  their  own  countries  could  never  sink  to  such  in-  famy,  because  the  innate  virtues  of  their  political  systems  would  make  it  impos-  sible,  but  it  is  worth  wondering  what  the  depression  of  1929  would  have  produced  here  if,  say,  the  [REDACTED]-  [REDACTED]  area  had  been  under  Ger-  man  military  occupation  for  a  decade,  [REDACTED]  had  been  through  a  Communist  revolution,  and  [REDACTED]  and  [REDACTED]  had  been  lopped  off  by  punitive  treaty-  makers.  Since  the  chill  wind  of  the  de-  pression  by  itself  was  enough  to  inflate  such  figures  as  Huey  Long  and  Father  Coughlin,  it  seems  permissible  to  sup-  pose  that  something  like  the  infinitely  worse  German  experience  would  have  had  much  more  profound  effects  on  the  national  behavior.  This  argument  in  no  way  justifies  the  Gestapo,  but  it  does  provide  a  more  profitable  line  of  approach  than  the  blanket  indictment  and  condemnation  of  the  German  people  that  Mr.  Crank-  shaw  is  now  repeating.  If  his  history  of  the  Gestapo  is  considered  as  the  case  history  of  a  disease  that  can  affect  any  society,  it  yields  some  valuable  lessons.  Department  IA  was  an  ideal  instrument  for  the  Nazis,  because  fear  had  made  the  internal  enemies  of  the  German  Repub-  lic  seem  more  powerful  than  they  were. The  mean  ing  of  a  ll  t  his  was  s  ummarize  d  by  the  historian  Grot  e  :  The  lawgiver  is  the  supreme  and  exclu-  sive  authority,  spiritual  as  well  as  secular.  No  dissenters  from  the  orthodoxy  pre-  scribed  by  him  are  admitted.  Those  who  believe  more  than  he  does,  and  those  who  believe  less,  however  blameless  their  con-  duct,  are  condemned  alike  to  pass  through  a  long  solitary  imprisonment  to  execution.  Not  only  the  speculations  of  enquiring  in-  dividual  reason  but  also  the  spontaneous  inspirations  of  religious  disquietude  or  terror  are  suppressed  and  punished.  The  dream  of  purchasing  social  stability  at  this  price  is  thus  at  least  twen-  ty-three  centuries  old.  It  has  been  toyed  with  by  ambitious  and  unscrupulous  men  ever  since,  and  in  every  form  of  so-  cial  organization.  It  has  always  been  waiting,  in  the  darker  recesses  of  the  human  mind,  for  the  moment  when  confusion,  exhaustion,  and  fear  allow  the  dream  to  be  turned  into  a  pattern  for  action. I  Turn  to  page  106  |  THE  NEW  43616e616461ER  stress  situation:  the  type  of  homogeneous  city-state  he  liked  was  showing  itself  unable  to  exist  in  the  face  of  a  new  kind  of  cosmopolitan  state.  Rather  than  see  what  he  loved  swept  away,  he  proposed  to  hold  up  the  march  of  history  with  a  program  of  terrorism  and  cynically  in-  vented  lies.  'The  events  narrated  in  &quot;Gestapo&quot;  are  by  no  means  unique.  Similar  stress  situations  have  always  pro-  duced  similar  programs,  and  they  have  grown  more  acute  than  ever  in  the  last  century  and  a  half,  in  which  obsolete  national  states  have  sought  to  perpetuate  themselves  in  an  increasingly  integrated  world  economy.  'The  voicesof  the  racists  have  become  more  strident  and  their  ideas  more  absolute  as  the  concep-  tion  of  mankind  as  an  entity  has  grown  in  clarity,  and  the  state's  demand  for  complete  loyalty  has  grown  more  in-  tense  as  the  concept  of  the  self-sufficient  nation  has  become  more  obviously  inane.  The  Gestapo's  destructive  fanaticism  was  directly  related  to  a  futile  effortto  maintain  Frederick  the  Great's  eight-  eenth-century  ideas  of  [REDACTED]  and  Germanism  in  a  twentieth-century  Europe;  the  organization  was  as  much  concerned  with  attempting  to  liquidate  history  as  it  was  with  liquidating  men,  and  with  forcing  the  community  and  the  world  to  accept  simple,  time-tested  answers  toentirely  new  and  complex  problems.  It  is  not  in  the  end  the  Ger-  mans  as  Germans  who  are  to  be  feared,  as  Mr.  Crankshaw  believes;  it  is  the  peo-  ple  in  every  country  who  consider  them-  selves  men  of  gold  and  produce  the  sweeping  solutions  and  programs  that  turn  into  nightmares  of  compulsion  and  brutality  in  the  hands  of  the  men  of  sil-  ver.  The  only  defense  against  them  is  the  society  conceived  in  terms  of  open  debate,  in  which  every  citizen  is  edu-  cated  to  form  his  views  and  to  express  them,  and  in  which  thereare  no  dark  corners  where  the  poisonous  weeds  of  arbitrary  and  absolute  power  can  grow.  -AxtHony  [REDACTED]  BRIEFLY  NOTED  FICTION  A  Famicy  Party,  by  John  O'Hara  (Random  House). The  author's  careful,  low-keyed  prose  makes  his  rather  sensational  material  (the  Taoudéni-  Timbuctoo  caravan  route  is  generally  regarded  as  the  worst  in  the  desert)  life-size  and  credible.  Highly  rec-  ommended.  There  are  maps  by  the  author,  and  many  photographs.  Tur  Tupors,  by  Christopher  Morris  (Macmillan).  An  essay  on  the  five  [REDACTED]  monarchs-those  hard,  flam-  boyant,  extravagant,  and  creative  kings  and  queens  under  whom  Eng-  land  became  a  great  power  and  mis-  tress  of  the  seas,  as  well  as,  paradox-  ically,  insular.  The  author,  who  is  a  lecturer  in  history  at  [REDACTED]  Uni-  versity,  believesthat  people  in  six-  teenth-century  [REDACTED]  were  differ-  ent  from  people  today,  and  instead  of  trying,  asso  many  biographers  do,  to  &quot;humanize&quot;  or  modernize  his  char-  acters,  he  looks  at  them  in  terms  of  their  own  time.  The  first  of  the  Tu-  dor  line,  Henry  VII,  whose  claim  to  the  throne  was  dubious  (he  said  he  was  obviously  king  because  he  was  wearing  the  crown),  is  treated  more  charitably  than  usual.  Then  come  Henry  VIII,  about  whom  not  much  new  is,  or  could  be,  said;  the  preco-  cious  Edward  VI,  who  died  at  fif-  teen;  Mary,  who  seems  to  have  been  a  wholly  unhappy  woman;  and  Eliz-  abeth,  who,  the  author  says,  &quot;had  something  nearer  genius  than  any  earlier  or  later  occupant  of  her  throne.&quot;  All  in  all,  an  engrossing  book,  enhanced  by  some  unusual  por-  traits  that  give  a  good  idea  of  what  the  43616e616461s  must  have  really  looked  like.  Tur  Frowrrs  or  Tur  ForEsT,  by  David  Garnett  ([REDACTED],  Brace).  In  this  volume,  which  is  the  second  installment  of  &quot;The  Golden  Echo,&quot;  the  gifted  son  of  a  celebrated  British  editor  and  critic  (Edward  Garnett)  and  of  the  pioneer  English  translator  of  Turgenev,  Dostoevski,  and  Che-  khov  (Constance  Garnett)  continues  the  gently  candid  and  enormously  en-  gaging  narrative  of  his  development  as  a  man,  as  an  individual,  and  as  an  artist.
